SATURDAY, MAY 21, 1892.
The Superfiuous Australian Infant.
In every city all the world over — even in the
new cities of Australia which have not yet had
time to settle down into the ancient, mouldy,
antediluvian wretchedness of Europe—there is
a great, floating population which lives by no
recognised industry, and does nothing in parti-
cular of a lawful character —a strange and slimy
band that exists somehow, but how no one
exactly knows. Of this class are the forlorn
commission-agent who lurks mysteriously in a
dirty office, and has shiny streaks along the
seams of his black coat as if he had been walked
upon by snails; the undefinable person who
calls in and breathes the name of a horse in
one’s ear the week before the Melbourne Cup,
and seems to have no other means of support;
the small sharebroker with the illuminated
nose, who has no office, and lives apparently on
the pavement by the Stock Exchange; the
pony-racing man ; the unaccountable, gaunt
female of despairing aspect, who is always
looking for a situation as governess and never
finding it; the other female who wants to copy
documents in a world where nobody ever wants
any documents copied ; the individual who has
an infallible remedy, which can be heard of by
writing toa box at the post office; the woman who
wants a “ kind gentleman” to lend £3; the dis-
tracted party who desires to get rid of an infant
as per advertisement in the evening press ; and
the kind, motherly person who is willing to
adopt an infant—or forty infants if the chance
presents itself—at from 40s. to £5 a head, and
bring them up respectably and educate them,
and clothe them, and yet make a profit on the
transaction in some manner not fully specified.
This last person is probably the most mys-
terious of all, for not only is she without visible
moans of support, but she is without any visible
means of supporting the immense family which
she adopts so recklessly, and yet the dull, slow-
going law, with its leaden heel and a film over its
Argus eye, never—or hardly ever—takes any
notice of her.
* « «
The motherly person who wants to take in an
infant has her habitat, so far as New South
Wales is concerned, chiefly in the columns of
the metropolitan evening press. There are very
many of her anybody who advertises a loose
offspring with a rather larger premium than
usual will be promptly rushed by many
middle-aged or elderly females, who all desire
to yearn over an uncared-for child for a cash
consideration. They are mostly a frowsy,
strong-minded crowd, and their business is
principally among that class of “ unmarried
mothers ” who possess a little money —the one
who has no money leaves her infant on the road,
or in a paddock, where it dies and is afterwards
discovered by a policeman, and inquested. Out-
side the “ Wanted ” column of the evening
press, their trade is mostly done in
those mysterious establishments where a
“ qualified person” furnishes ladies with
“ first-class accommodation.” At those places
a shady doctor is generally on hand to
prevent unnecessary publicity—an individual
whose great merit is that he sees nothing save
what he is paid to see, and says nothing, and
never makes any trouble. Nobody, as a rule,
registers the births which take place in those
establishments,and the“ kind,motherly person”
takes the superfluous infants away by contract,
with a reduction upon taking a quantity, and
after that they are never heard of again. It is
one of the great features of the business, in
fact, that no infant ever will be heard of again.
Moreover, it would be difficult to hear of them,
even if anyone attempted to do so. The name-
less children who are removed by contract from
those mysterious institutions already mentioned
presently get mixed with the ones that Avere
got by advertisement in the evening publication,
and which possibly were possessed of names,
only all memory of them was presently lost in
■the general confusion. Also, the ones that are
dead get mixed with the ones that are only
dying ; and those that are buried, and those
that are merely taken out and dropped in a
paddock, and those that are taken at £5 and
then traded away again or sub-let at 405., all
got confused together, and when it is necessary
to supply a name—which doesn’t very often
happen—any name serves the purpose. Conse-
quently, the chance of a child that is once
adopted by the “ motherly person ” of the daily
paper advertisement ever turning up again to
trouble anybody is exceedingly limited.
* # #
The practitioner who lives entirely by this
business—and there are some who do so, as
well as many who make it an adjunct to other
trades—must necessarily adopt about one infant
every fortnight to make her occupation even
moderately profitable. This calculation allows
nothing for board and lodging, clothing, educa-
tion, firing, and attendance—it is based on the
impossible assumption that the average child
goes naked, and never cats anything, and wants
no care, no education, and no attendance what-
soever. On this basis the owner of the estab-
lishment could live, and no more. Also, by this
same calculation, after, say, three years of the
trade, she would have a family of 78 infants
with a piospect of indefinite increase. As no
one, either in the trade or out. of it, has such a
family, and as the entire income of the estab-
lishment all this time would probably only
average some 50s. to £5 per week, it is obvious
that the adopter of human waifs and strays
must live by murder pure and simple. If she
let her stock accumulate it would mean absolute
financial ruin. Now and then some unfortunate,
who is unable to provide for her child, and
either cannot pay for the official assassination
or is too humane to trust the work to anybody
else, does her own infanticide, and probably
gets from three to five years in gaol as a conse-
quence ; but for some unexplained reason the
law, which drops so heavily on the woman who
commits murder from pressure of necessity,
takes very little cognisance of the one
who commits murder for £5. Sometimes,
as already mentioned, the latter simply deposits
her charge behind a fence, and leaves it
there to die from exposure. Sometimes she
leases it out for a few shillings to some sub-
ordinate practitioner who does the work at
cheap Chinese rates —the latter being generally
a person who is so drunken, or so tattered, or
so forbidding of expression and broken about
tire nose that she is unable to establish a con-
nection of her own. (generally, however, she
simply plants the adopted son or daughter in a
damp cellar, and in a few days matters arrange
themselves without any unpleasant publicity.
Then, in some cases, a discredited quack
supplies the necessary certificate testifying that
the deceased succumbed to inflammation—the
said certificate being one of a large quantity
that are furnished by the pound, and a cheap
undertaker, with a large connection among
similar establishments, either buries the
deceased or disposes of it in some other way—
possibly unauthorised cremation. At other
times, the deceased simply goes amissing, and is
never accounted for. And in still more cases,
there is no doctor, no certificate, and no under-
taker at all, and where the infant goes to,
whether dead or alive, is left a mystery. It
came into the world unrecorded, and it went
out the same way, and left no footmarks in an
Australian city that could be afterwards identi-
fied.
* * *
Once, and only once, so far as we know, w T as
an effort made to run the trade upon humane
lines, and, as the natural and inevitable result,
the operator got into gaol for a lengthened
term. Being constitutionally averse to infanti-
cide, and recognising the impossibility of rearing
and educating forty children for a lump sum of
£2OO or thereabouts, this lady adopted the
device of collecting t he infant and the money in
the evening, then deposited the former the same
night on the door step of some affluent citizen
at Toorak or St. Kilda, rang the bell furiously
to draw attention to the lost atom of humanity,
and ran for her life. Property-, how'ever, was
much outraged at having so many small, un-
considered trifles thus left on its hands, and
when she was captured and brought before the
Melbourne courts, her sentence was considerably
heavier than thatof the otherhired parent—tried
the other day in the same city—who got rid
of her encumbrance by gradual starvation,
and didn’t distress any capitalist by leaving it
at his door. But even the latter individual—
who got only nine pionths —was much more
unfortunate than the majority of her class, for
ninety-nine per cent, of the practitioners in this
branch of industry go on their way year after
year, and attract no attention whatsoever.
They are the only variety of assassins who ven-
ture to advertise their business openly in the
papers. The advertisement of the baby-farmer
reads almost undisguisedly to the effect that
anybody who is too young and helpless to resist
will he quietly and punctually abolished for a
small fee, and never heard of again. And this
variety of assassination is many shades more
criminal than the ordinary homicide, because it is
done by slow torture, ill-usage, and hunger instead
of by the prompt and comparatively painless
methods of the commonplace Thug —who is
punctually hanged for his doings. Also it is
committed neither under the influence of drink,
jealousy, anger, a sense of injury, or any other
human emotion which could possibly be termed
an extenuating circumstance ; it is a cold,
callous, and utterly mercenary crime done for a
few shillings, and it presents not one redeeming
feature to save its perpetrator from the gallows,
so long as the gallon's is a legal institution.
Yet no Australian jury ever finds the criminal
—on the rare occasions when the criminal is
found at all—guilty of anything worse than
manslaughter, and the penalty inflicted, as a
rule, is a little more than that awarded for
vagrancy, and a little less than that allotted
for petty larceny.
* # *
Last session the New South Wales Legislature
passed a Bill for the greater protection
of infants, whereby a number of elaborate
safeguards were built up. But notwith-
standing this, the same old harridan still
advertises that she will take the customary
infant at the same old price, and the early
spring orphan is hawked round on the half-shell
as heretofore. And so long as the inevitable
advertisement appears in the evening paper
setting forth that a kind motherly person is
willing to undertake the lifelong charge of any
new-born inhabitant, and furnish him or her
with board, lodging, clothing, education, &c.,
for a sum ranging from 40s. to £lO, it remains
a matter of certainty that the law is inoperative
and a failure. Probably the weakest point of
every Act dealing with this subject is that it
does not absolutely prohibit all such advertise-
ments and subject any newspaper proprietor
publishing them to a heavy penalty. The law
already recognises that the process of infant-
fanning is murder almost undisguised; the
farmer’s “ ad.” is a public incitement to murder,
and the publication thereof is~something which
need not be more definitely specified. Also the
law furnishes no alternative to the present
system, for reasons which are sufficiently
obvious. The only alternatives are the establish-
ment of a State institution where nil super-
fluous offspring will be taken in without any
question whatsoever, or the legalisation of a
painless method of infanticide, and the Legisla-
ture lacks the moral courage to face cither
solution of the problem.
Queen Victoria’s Birthday.
One day next week Queen VICTORIA intends
having a birthday, and Australia will keep
holiday in honour of the event, and rejoice
officially with an exceeding great official joy
over its monarch’s natal day. Hardly anybody
in Australia has ever seen Queen Victoria,
but presumably there is such a person, and
with a view to avoiding circumlocution and an
undue waste of pronouns, The Bulletin
generally takes her for granted, and speaks of
her as an ascertained fact. Still she only
exists, for us, upon hearsay evidence, and her
reality has never been absolutely proved. The
world reverenced William Tell for several
centuries, and raised monuments to him, and
wrote his history, and then found out at last
that there had never been such a person. It
has gone in mortal fear of the Devil for 1900
years, and has prayed to be delivered from
him, and has seen him on dark nights holding
orgies with witches and other unholy persons,
and now it is just beginning to realise that
there never was any Devil after all. What the
world knows of Victoria is chiefly that she is
pre-eminent in grief and reigns by the Grace of
Heaven, and as the world is growing more and
more doubtful in this age of dull materialism
that there is a Heaven, the statement rests on
an insecure foundation. The outward evidences
of Victoria’s existence are, principally, the
occasional apparition of a closed carriage or
donkey-cart somewhere in the United Kingdom,
and a hazy allegation that Victoria is inside.
Those who are on the spot only see the vehicle ;
those who are not on the spot only hear that
some alleged persons are alleged to have seen
it. So far as this evidence goes, the genuine
monarch may have died 20 years ago, or may
never have been existent. The Turkish Empire
was ruled for three months by the outside
aspect of a tent with the dead body of Sulvman
the Magnificent inside, and in like manner the
British Empire may be run by the outside of a
shabby vehicle with a myth and a baseless
allegation within. The British nation whoops
for the outside of that vehicle, and grovels
before it, and takes the contents for granted,
and in a certain sense it is wise in doing so. If
there never has been any Victoria at all, but
only some one who pretended to bo her, or only
a general impression that there was someone,
the country gets there just the same. The
English people were much excited at one time
about the doings of a monarch who reigned
over a kingdom that could never be found on a
certain floating island in the Atlantic; and
between the non-existent person who is sup-
posed to be the sovereign of a real country and
the non-existent person who is supposed to rule
an unreal one, there is not much to choose.
# # #
Assuming, however, without any sufficient
evidence, that Victoria is a reality, her birth-
day is going to be celebrated next week—just
as William Tell’s birthday was celebrated
till it was discovered that he never had one.
The reason of the celebration is not very clear.
The English-speaking races, in general, do not
propose to rejoice publicly because Victoria,
who was very, very old twelve months ago, is
now twelve months older. There would be a
depth of cold-blooded meanness in such a pro-
ceeding that even a very mean nation would
hesitate to openly acknowledge. Neither are
they going to rejoice, because, though she is
very old, yet she isn’t so old as she might be ;
the people who could get up much public
enthusiasm over that kind of negative blessing,
would fire off guns because a monarch wasn’t
bald. They do not intend to exuberate because
Victoria isn’t dead, or if they do, the process
is decidedly illogical; for Victoria is alive all
the year round, and if the empire is so glad
that she isn’t dead for one day out of the 365
that it must fire guns and keep holiday over the
circumstance—its utter indifference for the
other 364 days is unaccountable. The devotion
which consists in being glad that its subject is
alive for one day in the year, is very near akin
to the malevolence which wants her to be
dead all the rest of the time. Moreover, if
the festival is based on the mere fact that
she is alive, then Victoria should let off
crackers in her own imperial back yard, and
fire guns herself, and hold her own festival, for
however glad the empire might be that she still
holds out there seems no reason to doubt that
she is very much gladder herself. This remark
applies not only to Victoria but to humanity
in general. However pleased the world may
feel that anybody isn’t dead it isn’t half so well
pleased as the individual himself. Owing to a
certain mystery which hangs over the hereafter
almost everybody is pleased at not being dead,
and the only known exceptions to this rule are
those who would have been much better pleased
if they had never been alive.
# * *
Practically, therefore, the only feasible or
possible explanation is that the English-speak-
ing races propose to rejoice over the bald cir-
cumstance that Victoria has a birthday. As
everybody has a birthday, and as Victoria, in
common with the rest of humanity, didn’t
achieve her anniversary, but had it thrust upon
her in the ordinary, common, irresponsible
way, and as her birthday is of no use to her, any
more than anybody elsc’s birthday is to its
owner, and as she couldn’t help it, and is no
better for it, and as the mere fact of being born
is but a questionable blessing at the best, this
explanation is an exceedingly lame and unsatis-
factory one, but it is the most promising that
presents itself. Yet Victoria, like all the rest
of her kind, was only an accidental circumstance
after all. The whole human race suffers from this
same disadvantage. It is an accidental creation
arising from the purely accidental marriage of
two individuals who met by chance, and were
governed throughout by all manner of external
influences. If they had not met each other
then, through some other accident, they would
probably each have met and married someone
else. Under certain given conditions Victoria
was born to inherit the British crown, and he
grovelled to by her subjects. Under other con-
ditions some one would have been born, but
w hether it would have been Victoria or not is
an unsolved problem. In the one ease it would
be interesting to know where Victoria would
have been, or if she would ever have been at
all ; in the other it would be equally interesting
to know where the other individual is, or
wdiether that other individual is at all. It is
one among the exceedingly few certainties of
life that many millions of the human species
have been born by accident; it is another of
them that, also by accident, many more millions
have somehow missed being born at all. And
even of these two bare and unsatisfactory facts
the first, at least, has been called in question, for
more than one philosopher has arisen to prove
that no person has ever been born or lived or
died at all, that there is nothing and nobody
and noplace to put him if there was anybody,
that there is neither time nor space nor any-
thing else, and that everything is a delusion and
a blank. Among all these conflicting problems
it is difficult to place even the Queen’s Birthday
on a satisfactory footing. And, perhaps it is
for some or all of these reasons, or possibly it is
for the purpose of disguising its emotion,
that the great Anglo-Saxon race generally gets
drunk on this portentous occasion, and wakes
up with a swollen head next day, and then
hangs up the conundrum still unsolved for
another twelve months. In the present instance
it will doubtless get drunk as usual, and some
of it will suicide in the nearest river, and some
will hang itself on a convenient tree, and a part
of it will be fined for bounding on a policeman,
and it will be upset in the harbour, and have its
corns trodden on in the crush at the railw ay-
stations, and in various other ways it will take
its pleasure about as sadly as a consumptive
rejoicing in an hospital, or a solitary prisoner
holding a lonesome revel on hominy in his cell.
And as for the cause of all this festivity, she
will doubtless celebrate in her accustomed man-
ner by visiting the damp tombs of her dead
relations, for she also takes her pleasures
solemnly, and is fond of woe and fiost and
hearses, and similar symbols of a depressed
spirit and a pre-eminence in grief.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Diseased Meat.
Here is an interesting extract from the evi-
dence of a Melbourne butcher, who lately went
the way of all meat—into the Insolvency
Court :
lie used to kill cattle for sale in Paddy’s Market, but
the inspector would condemn his moat nearly every day.
He never could get any good cattle. The “ toil ” butchers
used to secure them, and he had to buy the “rough
’uns.” He had to get a few good cattle to hang in front
of his shop, and this enabled him to sell the bad stuff
which he had at the back. He continually changed the
names of his shops in order to prevent people from know-
ing that the inspector had condemned the meat, and
every time the meat was condemned he put a new name
over the shop and a new man in charge of it. lie could
not say when ho was first prosecuted for selling goals for
lamb, but it was when he was in 36(5 Brunswick-street,
about six months after- he started. His business never
recovered, but he still kept it on. He was prosecuted for
selling unwholesome meat lots of times.
If this evidence proves anything at all it
demonstrates that the butcher who scatters
disease and death among his customers isn’t fined
often enough or heavily enough. The meat in
the present case was condemned “lots of times,”
and the vendor was prosecuted over and over
again, but still lie went on his way and'sold his
loathsome flesh just as before. Evidently, there-
fore, the law was not strong enough to stop him
in his death-dealing course in fact, it would
seem that it paid him better to pay the
fines and go right on selling flukey chops and
gangrened steak than to deal in good meat and
not pay any fines. It was only when he
began to trade in goat -probably in wholesome
goat, for that animal is seldom diseased—
that Nemesis overtook him Unluckily, too (


jhe insolvent under notice is not a solitary
butcher, but a member of a very large and
flourishing class. There are a dozen such
butchers in every Australian city. They may
be known generally by the big tickets on their
heap joints, and by the blackened carcases
that arc festering on the slabs inside the shop,
I the gaunt and hungry-looking women
who paw afc these carcases, and try to pick out
the least offensive one that they may get their
dinner off it, and the bloated sausages that have
rrreen spots on them in some places and black
spots in others, and the air of old age
which pervades the cheap provender gener-
ally. The trade is a profitable one, and
s the law never sends the butcher to
oaol for six months ho simply pays his fine
at regular intervals, and keeps on selling con-
sumptive flesh just as before. There is more
sudden death in the shop of one low-grade
butcher than in six average grog-shanties, but
the butcher isn’t a licensed practitioner in
meat, and so long as he likes to stand the occa-
sional consequences no one prevents him
selling his devastating food till the end of
time. Moreover, even the “respectable”
butcher invariably has his wares exposed in an
unglazed shop front, so that the dust and pollu-
tion of the streets settle upon them and make
them foul, and his carcases are hung low down
so that dogs can sniff at them, and lean up
against them, and slaver hydatids upon them,
auk the sun shines upon them, and the rain
rains on them, and they get the full benefit of
the smoke and the sewer-gas ; and every kind
of infection that is going attaches itself to them.
If the butcher had any sense of the fitness of
things his sign would often be a skeleton with
a scythe in one hand and a leg of beef in the
other, and round its bony neck would be
festooned a pound of sausages as an emblem of
the wrath that is to come.
The W.S.W. Religious Census.
The religious census of New South Wales, the
results of which were published last week, is a
fearful and wonderful document. Among the
one-and-a-quarter million inhabitants of the
province there arc 190 different creeds and
sects, many of which tail off mysteriously into
sub-creeds and odd fragments of sects ; and in
addition to all these there are a large number
who return themselves as belonging to “no
religion another large number who have a
religion but no denomination ; a lot more who
are put down as “unspecified or ill-defined,”
and over 11,000 who stand out altogether, and
refuse to commit themselves. No less than 59
religions have only one supporter apiece, a soli-
tary individual who expects to go to Heaven all
alone, while all the rest of the nation falls with
a thud into perdition. A striking feature just
here is that, in only six instances, is the lone-
some apostle a woman, and the six women who
hold up six deserted creeds all by themselves
appear to be mostly Scotch. Evidently the
average religious female wants a parson to lean
upon, and when she forms a church by herself,
she finds the solitude too much for her. Taking
them all round, the religionists of New South
Wales are a humorous gathering. There are
four “ Calithumpians ” among them, and one
sad and friendless person who puts himself
down as an “ Admirer of Nature,” one “ Pessi-
mist,” and only three who record themselves
as “ Unprejudiced.” Probably three unpre-
judiced Christians out of one-and-a-quarter
millions are all that could reasonably be ex-
pected. There is one—only one “ Believer in
Facts,” and one “ Brotherhood of Man ’’ indi-
vidual with nobody in all the rest of the list to
be a brother to him, also one “ Moralist,” one
“No-Christian,” and a single “Seeker,” one
“Philosopher,” one “ Bellamyite,” and—sad,
unfriended, melancholy, slow, at the tail-end of
the list —a dejected “Fatalist.” Also, there is
one “Open Brethren, ’ which is a cheerful
thing in a land where most of the brethren
seem to be shut fast; and along with these
fanev and comic believers there are a lot of
hard” - shell sectarians who put themselves
down as “ Ecclesiastes VI. only,” “ Ephesians
11., 8,” “Gathered unto Me,” “Nur-
tured in the Admonition of the Lord,
“Hardshell Baptist,” “Do Good,” “Saints,”
and one tough individual who defines himself
as “ Experience.” It is a notaole circumstance
that the church which defines itself as “ Do
Good ” has just one follower. None of the others
say anything about doing good. Judging from
this column and a quarter of assorted cranks,
the women of New South Wales are by far the
more religious half of the community. Where-
cver some lonesome person comes down
with a crash and announces himself as the
proud owner of a particularly godless creed,
he always turns out to be a man.
The Seeker, the Eclectic individual, the
Materialistic Atheist, the Philosopher, the ISio
Christian, the Moralist, the Radical, the
Brotherhood of Man person, the Believer in
Facts, the Iconoclasts, the Positivists, the
Fatalist, the Bellamyite, the Sceptics, Rational-
ists, Nihilist, Non-Intrusionist, Evolutionist,
Natist, Admirer of Nature, Calithumpians, and
the dreary Pessimist—there is not a woman
among them all. Also the particularly self-
sufficient bigots—the Gathered unto Me crowd,
the Open Brother, and a lot more of the same
order—arc males all through. The women of
the community are solid upon the old, orthodox
beliefs and evidently don’t care to take any
risks, or to tool needlessly with the hereafter.
Here and there in the list some garish sect con-
sists of a man and a woman, but in these cases
it is safe to assume that the man invented the
sect and dragged his wife in after him. As for
the six women who have a separate creed apiece,
The Bulletin hereby bets that they are all
out-of-date, unmarriageable widows, and that
they only hang on out of regard for the old
man’s memory.
♦
The man who cables out the sick, old flap-
doodle for the Australian daily press wired
last week, at considerable expense, that “ the
Times states it has been unable to discover the
necessity of Mr. Dinns’ visit to England.” This
thrilling mass of information suggests several
solemn'’thoughts. (1) “The Times” sounds
very imposing, but it only means, after all,
Mr. Walter and another man, and the fact that
they have been unable to discover something,
is just as remarkable as the fact that Mr. Smith,
of Wilcannia, has been unable to find his dog. (2)
Nobody in Australia cares a straw about it, and
the sun will shine just the same, and the price of
beer won’t go up, and the circumstance will
make no difference whatsoever. (3) It is.
lawful for anybody to go where he pleases,
whether the Times sees a necessity for it or
not; for that matter, there are a good many
people who don’t see a necessity for the Times,
but they don’t send cablegrams about it. (4)
Even if the Times did see the necessity for the
visit, or if it saw the necessity for Dikes, or if
Dibbs saw the necessity for the Times, or if
Nebuchadnezzar saw the necessity for Samuel
Griffith, or if everybody saw the necessity for
everybody else, it doesn’t arouse the slightest
interest in anybody’s soul, and the man who
sends out such unspeakable twaddle ought to be
abolished as an outrage on common sense. (5)
Several other reflections arc held over for want
of space.

One day last week nine seamen — three
Swedes, two Frenchmen, two Norwegians,
and a pair of Finns, mostly very weak in their
knowledge of the English language, and
utterly guileless of Australian law — were
brought up at a Sydney police court, charged ,
with “disobedience to lawful orders.” What
the orders were was practically left un-
defined, but presumably they were lawful, more
or less. The captain —who did speak English—
was present to prosecute, and a solicitor was
present to help him. The nine defendants had ,
no solicitor, and they broke down their case at*
the very beginning by pleading “ guilty ” in i
nine different varieties of unintelligible English.
Whether they knew the meaning of the word,
or whether they knew what they were
pleading guilty to, or whether they really
meant to plead “ not guilty ” but got
hold of the wrong expression, and if so,
who supplied them with the expression, were
all points that were left uninvestigated. The
sentence was seven days in gaol, £2 to be stopped
from each man’s wages —presumably for the ,
owner’s benefit, 6s. IGd. each court costs, and !
21s. each for the solicitor, who thus made nine
guineas by sitting in court for ten minutes, and
saying nothing. That unhappy nomad, the
sailor, because he hasn’t, and never had, and
doesn’t seem likely to have a vote, is always
treated with this same extraordinary variety of
law, the like of which is served out to no other
individual under the sun. If he is brutally
kicked, and fed on decayed biscuit and
festering salt -Jiorse, he has practically
no redress, for the courts always look with
suspicion upon his statements, and besides, if
he goes on shore to look for redress, he is
generally arrested for being “ absent without
leave.” If he is unusually successful he may
possibly see a specially brutal skipper fined £5,
and he has the pleasing knowledge that that
potentate will kick him harder and supply him
with worse salt-horse all the return voyage, to
get level, and finish up by putting him in irons,
and charging him with “disobedience,” and
getting him gaoled, and his Avages forfeited. If
he refuses to go aboard again, in view of these
unpleasant possibilities, he is arrested and
fined, and put on board by main force.
There is no earthly reason why the sailor
who is absent from his work should be
gaoled, any more than the M.L.A., or the
Civil Service clerk, or the Premier, or the
Railway Commissioner, or the bishop, under
the same conditions ; only he has no vote, and
the law is built that way. Also the sailor who
disobeys some trivial order, so long as he does
not endanger life by so doing, is no more
criminal than any other employe, from the
Under-Secretary for the Treasury downwards,
who does exactly the same thing. The com-
plaint of the Shiels Government against the
autocratic Speight was that he never paid the
slightest attention to any orders, yet the idea
of having him “run in” didn’t occur even to
David Syme in his wildest dreams. If the
Australian Governments have any sense of jus-
tice they will look into this awful discrepancy
forthwith.
The Ethics of Protection.
As a prelude, we must define what we mean by
Protection, as now that this term is so much used
by the jugglers and bunglers who pose as political
leaders its precise meaning is becoming propor-
tionally obscure.
Let it be understood then that when we talk
about Protection we do not mean the tariff of
Treasurer See, of N.S. Wales, which, though it
maybe right enough as a start, is almost as far
from being protective as that which it has
replaced. Neither should we consider a penny in
the £ on the unimproved value of land the Single-
tax, though that also might be a step in the right
direction.
By Protection we mean the complete thing;
that is, duties sufficiently high to practically
induce the people of this country to produce for
themselves those commodities that could not be
made, mined, or grown with a less total expendi-
ture of human energy elsewhere.
Observe: we say nothing about <£ s. d. Our
standard of value, and the only one we recognise, is
the power' of human muscles guided by human
brains.
Labour men everywhere, no matter whether
they call themselves Freetraders or Protectionists,
are agreed that “ the product of labour is the
natural reward of that labour.”
This being conceded, we should, under a system
of complete Protection, obtain (nationally at least)
the natural reward of our labour in its product.
No just man wants more than this. For instance,
he does not want in exchange for it the product
of the labour of, say, a dozen Chinamen, who
have the misfortune to be owned by a thirteenth
man, who does nothing himself except to part
with the product of their painful toil for less than
equivalent, and as No. 13 gets his goods for
nothing, lie can well afford to be liberal in Ins
trading. Wo are going to assume, then, that the
Single-tax party is mainly composed of just men,
who do not want for their labour more than its
product or equivalent, i.e., the product of an
equal total amount of labour by other people,
whether they be citizens or aliens. This, then,
being the case, it is difficult to see why any demo-
crat should object to the principlo of Protection,
which, as already stated, only aims at preventing
the, competition of the slave-driving outsider.
Upon everv considerable part of the earth’s
surface is to be tomel everything in the way of
raw material required by uuu, and the tastes and
abilities of men are as varied in kind as these sanm
' natural resources. There is, therefore, no physical
• obstacle to prevent Australians attaining the
highest standard of civilisation and material well-
‘ being, even though every description of foreign
trade were destroyed,
Now, as no country has natural advantages,
superior to Australia’s, there is nothing to be
gained by an exchange of products with Europe,
provided the exchange is a fair one, and each side
gets full value. In fact, we not only gain nothing,
but lose on the transaction to the extent of freight
and importers’ and exporters’ charges. The exist-
ing trade between Australia and the rest of the
world is simply an arrangement whereby wo.
barter our raw materials for their manufactures.
Meanwhile we either rob or get robbed, or
conduct the business at a mutual loss. Now, if
wo arc to continue producers of raw materials
only, how are we to provide employment for all
our citizens?
At the present time our produce is practically
unsaleable, which indicates that there are too
many people here now for such a purpose.
If we are to continue producers of raw materials i
only we must got rid of our unemployed, and as i
there is nothing for them to do here, the logical
course would be either to kill them or send them
back to Europe to increase by their consumption .
there the volume of our export trade. Also, every
Australian child who manifested a preference for
manufacturing should either be slain or else torn
away from his Australian homo and sent to follow *
his natural calling amidst the smoky squalor of i
the Northern nations.
Trade cuts for itself deep channels, and along i
these channels it will continue to run whether for ;
weal or woe, until some gigantic effort is made to- -
divert it into new ones. Until this effort has |
been made by us we cannot have Australian
manufactures.
Protection, either in the form of Customs duties |
or direct prohibition, is the only lever with which |
the necessary force can be obtained to secure the J
diversion of manufacturing industries to Aus- j
tralia. -
If a man walking alongside a level line of rail- ;
way where some ten-ton trucks were being shunted
got behind one of the detached wagons before it had
quite stopped and shoved with a force of 801bs. he '
could keep the vehicle going so long as he chose to
apply his 801bs. pressure. Let him, however,
allow the waggon to come to a standstill and then ,
try to move it, he will have to push a long way f
harder than 801bs. before he stirs it an inch. This
is what mechanicians term the “Friction of Rest,”
or the extra force with which a stationary body
resists being moved as compared with the force
which the same body in motion would require to
be maintained in motion.
There is an exact parallel to this in political
economy. If we wish to start new industries we.
must be prepared to give them a handsome pre-
liminary shove-off —which means in practice a
tariff considerably higher than would be prohibi-
tive under normal conditions.
A tariff ceases to be a tax as soon as it becomes
protective, and areally protective tariff does not
rob the community, it only prevents the com-
munity from robbing the individual, particu-
larly the large community of non-producers
from preying upon the smaller community
of hona-fi.de wealth-producing workers by securing
them against iniquitous foreign competition.
Moreover, if any citizen considers himself robbed,
he is quite free to start competition with the
“ robber,” which he will do quickly enough if he
thinks it worth his while.
If the Australian workingman wants to pay
other people less for their labour than he expects
to receive for a similar quantity of his own, he is
morally as much a slave-owner as a Queensland
planter with his kidnapped Kanakas —and the
Australian capitalist -would be quite justified in
treating him with as little consideration as the
said working-man extended to other working-men
less fortunate than himself.
The most common objection Single-taxers have
to Protection is this. They say ; —“ Give us Land
Nationalisation and we won’t need Protection,
because we will be able to manufacture more
cheaply than any other people on the face of the
earth, be they bond or free.”
In reply to this stock argument it is sufficient
to say : “ If you will be able to do this, Protection
cannot hurt you, for the duties would practically
be a dead letter, as no one would be so foolish as
to send coals to Newcastle.” “Help us to get
Protection and we will help you to obtain Land
Nationalisation.”
Moreover, capitalists as a class directly or
indirectly enjoy the economic advantage of free
land, and foreign capitalists so circumstanced
would have to be competed with even if our own
territory were nationalised, unless we had Protec-
tion in addition.
An experienced Sydney publican says that
trust results not only in a loss of beer, but in a
loss of custom as well. In most cases, a man who
“ goes on the slate ” is never seen again. It is
usual “ in the trade,” when the impecunious one
—if he be a decent man—asks credit, for Bung to
say, “ I don’t trust, but I’ll give it to you.” Bung
understands the art of throwing a sprat to catch
a m ackeral.
Morris Hepner, of Dunedin (M.L.), died
the other day with a newspaper in his hand. He
had been reading a brutal assault trial, which
broke off abruptly just at the most interesting
part, when a witness was testifying how the
plaintiff had been banged with a shovel, the court
having adjourned for the day. Then the old man
lifted up his voice querulously and stated that
they shouldn’t knock off like that, as a fellow
might never live to read the other instalment of
the story, and with that he fell on the floor and
died, lie’ll read the balance of that assault case
in a better world.
Dear Bulletin, —Absurd to talk about
settling the people on the land when a follow has
to fight two, and sometimes half-a-dozen squatters
and a host of jackeroos. When Cocky lias done
his five years the boss comes along and offers him
a few shillings per acre—a price, perhaps, that
wouldn’t keep a Chinaman in rice for six months.
Sometimes Cocky won’t “ bite ” at the price—
result; that locality is straightway seized with a
violent attack of spontaneous combustion.
The look-out man reports the run on fire,
and two squatters are promptly in the
saddle and on the spot. Cocky is out
shooting scrub-wallabies; he sees the smoke
and is soon battling with the flames, doing
his best to save Lis bit of grain. The squatter,
too, lends a hand ;so do his men. After all is put
out, they accuse Cocky of firing the grass, and in a
few days’ time he, in answer to a summons, finds
himself before a P.M. and three bulky squatters,
charged with combusting Swindlurn run. He is
duly committed to stand his trial at next Circuit
Court, which will just give him time to mortgage
his selection to provide money for his defence.
Possibly ho gets off. If he does, he isn’t able to
pay interest on money borrowed, so he is presently
sold up and the squatter t ikes the lot. We’re a
wonderful people, we are- Yours, Onthewallabv.

Dear Moorabinua,—
Judging from the many stories one hears of the gilded
youths foaming at the mouth over the neat way in which
Angelina has choused them out of their love-gifts, I am
beginning to think that the engaged young man ought
never to give a present, only a specification. For
instance, Romeo might say, “ Here, my darling, is the
rear and front elevation of a diamond ring I will give you
when we are married,” or “ How do you like the ground-
plan of this ruby bangle, dnckey ? It’s yours the day you
become Mrs. Dash,’ and the girl would hang on to him
like grim death—or even it she didn’t there wouldn’t be
Such unseemly back talk over present-returning as there
is now. According to etiquette, the gentleman cannot
ask for ’em back if his life depended upon it, but from
what I see of it they do everything but ask. The latest
wail is from a badly-plundered southern Edwin. His
ex-girl, in response to a letter demanding them all back,
arranged his love-tokens in artistic groups, had them
photographed and sent him, a copy !' And he keeps the
picture framed and shows it to all and sundry. What
congenial couple they might have made!
Sydney is essentially* a free-show city, and it is aston-
ishing how* big a party* a typical Mrs. So-and-so rakea
together when she is invited to. bring it on a card—con-
sequently, Miss Dora pianoforte-recital
op Thursday* last, at \. M.O. A.,was asfullas Heaven ought
to be by now. Miss B, was assisted, by charming Mrs.
Yanderveer Greene, in bright array—cream satin and
aged pearls, with hip-bouquet of autumn leaves—who,
sang a sleepy song or two very sweetly, but she really
should n t say* “deay” for “day*,” or “angils” for
angels. Dgslouis, with a white boutonniere (what a
festh e thing is a boutonniere on a Frenchman ! whereas
the beflowered Englishman has the same emotional effect
upon the beholder as has a parsley-garnished cold cut
joint) sang an aria from Le Roi de Lahore like an artist,
a..d refused the warm encore, and Kowalski was also on
deck. Viewed in the bpny lights that beat about a
ppblic pianiste it becomes plain that Miss Baggd’s music
will never make the trees dance—but the stage was
beautifully decorated with potted plants, and “she ” was
almost smothered in flowers. “ She ought to be satisfied,”
said somebody as the evening progressed, and really
When you come to think of it that is the most good-
humoured point of view to. regal’d the amateur recital
from, isn’t it ?
l?ut yet I, must rise to remonstrate with these gifted
amateurs who take halls and invite criticism, because
they* are in danger of becoming a nuisance. Now-a-days,
if a gill fiddles, or squalls, or thumps just decently*, she
takes a hall and invites the public to see her do it. And we
hearken and wonder and weep while the star of the evening,
with her longest-tailed gown on, and powder between her
slioulder-blades, undulates among the ferns and fairy
lamps, and apes the artist in everything but execution.
The lady who figures in front of every* one of her friends
entirely to her own satisfaction, doesn’t know how unreal
and ridiculous, the whole thing seems to be to a pensive
outsider. Admiring friends are dreadful impostors.
The lawn-tennis tournament, came to an end on Sydney
Ass. Ground on 14th inst., and Lady* Jersey, in silver
hncade, trimmed with grey feathers, front of folded grey
chiffon, and a bonnet not at all en suite, gave the guer-
dons away to the winners at the close of the afternoon.
Apropos, when Her Ex. stood bolt upright at the top of
the steps, while a hoy rang a big auction-bell to attract
tjie crowd, it really looked as if that gracious personage
was going for what she would fetch. It only needed the
presence of a dark fat man with a hook nose and a white
waistcoat, to make one believe her ladyship was about to be
sold without reserve. The prizes were the usual things
silver mugs, and so on, and it was deeply amusing to see
how the champions took their laurels. The women were
most natural under the trying circumstances—women
always are when it’s a matter of publicly posing, but
the bowing of the male element argued that tennis
doesn’t bring out a man’s gracefulness, nor teach him
not to turn his toes in. One tall, thin champion—
who went off with a big silver quart measure in either
hand looking for all the world as if he were an industrious
pot-boy*— stumbled up the steps to receive his blushing
honours with his tongue out like an ant-eater-but if
people are embarrassed by Lady Jersey it is their own
fault, for she certainly has the knack of prize-giving. You
couldn’t imagine a more re-assuring manner.
Most of the women at the tennis final had evidently
used two mirrors in dressing, and the general effect was
frbbk'y. I spotted out what I, believe was the prettiest
wbman there in a rich robe of brown corduroy*, velvet
toque trimmed with mushroom bengaline and topped off
with brown, and wide-feathered brown hat; somebodyin
snuff-brown made one wood r why the woman with a hen
profile trims herself with hen-feathers ? One may he too
eii suite, remembah. And other people forced me to the
eohdusion that the monkey-faced woman is a fool to trim
her long coat-tails with fur—it reminds one of the atavis-
tic’theory, for one thing. And the girl with the poodle
face, who wears a leather collar to her gown, does so at
hi?r own risk.

The bazaar was very dreamily draped, and the stalls
looked an artistic jumble of bright colours. Pin-cushions
were the pieces of resistance, and some of the designs
wore fairly new. The first prize for originality was given to
a doll arranged on a fizz-bottle. It was acharming dol! in
white satin as “Queen of Hearts,” but T hardly saw
where the originality of the pin-cushion came in, except
that mannikin pins were stuck round appliqu6 hearts
from whence it would require a Chinese length of nail to
dislodge them. I liked a little skeleton with a black net
inside which ran up to its skull and you stuck pins into
his cerebrum. A stuffed kid glove with pins in the palm
was horrible—a woman who could stick pins into a thing
like that would stick a pin into her husband ; and another
on the ghastly side was a coffin you raised the lid
and stuck pins into the stuffed effigy of a corpse ! The
tiny cushion must have needed deft fingers, but other-
wise the idea was foolish, and one cake-shaped cushion
was inscribed “Blessed are the merciful’’—for they shal 1
give you a pin, I suppose.
I am sorry I missed the amateur Shakesperean entertain-
ment in aid of Sydney Women's College, for, I hear, it was a
great success. “Juliet ” and “Katherine” (in Henry Vee)
played by the same pretty girl, were quite too pleasing, I
am told, if an applauding audience is anything to go upon,
Butdoesn’t it seem rather Sisyphus-like—though it must be
dreadfully necessary—this ceaseless toil of entertainments
in the cause of the Women’s College bag? The two greatest
working expenses—principal’s screw and house-rent—
together amount to £Bo} per annum, and to pay that off
there are only three students as you might put it-one of
whom is located in the Women’s College for the same
reason that an everyday person puts a china egg under
the hen. Now, viewed with the cold-boiled optic of icy
calculation, doesn’t it seem like great waste of nervous
energy for various nice, if faddy, people to go bald worry,
iug about making that £BOO a sure and certain debt just
so that three charming young lady students shan’t be
boarded out ? Anybody would think the Women’s
College board was composed of philanthropic mashers!
Of course, the said board could soon have S.R.O,
hanging gracefully from the bell-pull if they liked
to lower their terms, but they mean to keep the
“ fee ’ up far above the means of the girl who can’t
afford to ti’ot up to her “classes” in a private hansom,
and while they keep it up I’m afraid their enthusiastic
admirers will have to fly to Shakespeare many many
times. The College can’t pay for its own oats with only'’
three bags of grist in the mill. This may be a doubtful
metaphor, but it is simply beautiful sense.

There is always much pow-wow and talkee among the
mixed members of the Sydney School of Arts Library,
and it’s always the women who shriek loudest. But I

Even aldermen (I have never seen one weighing less
than fifteen stun) play kittenish pranks when they are off
on a picnic-tour. A correspondent tells of tricks played
in a Southern town recently. One wag directed a jovinj
city father (after the banquet) to the wrong bed-chamber,
which, of course, wasn’t unoccupied, and the alderman
fled in his socks. Another mad wag substituted a po-
mauteau full of lady’s lingerie tor the traps of a pompon*
magnate who was howling in a dressing-gown until half-
an-honr before the banquet. But cannot the Town Hall
give us something new by way of a joke? The world is
so tired of the wrong-door and the ruffled petticoats.
Have you heard the latest little blackmail story ? They
were two rich men and they went South without their
wives, and while away they did an innocent act of kind-
ness to a few hard-working girls and quite forgot to men-
tion it when they got home. But the other day when one
of them threatened to sue a well-known slippery foreigner
for several hundred pounds, money lent, he vowed he
would tell the whole Arcadian story to their wives if they
proceeded against him ! Consequently the summons
wasn’t issued. It was only an innocent act of kindness,
of course, but the world is so given to wink its other eye,
Married “society” men on their travels should beware
of that slippery foreigner—if all I bear is true. Once he
invited a couple of roving Benedicts to a little supper to
meet some lady friends. The lady friends all had golden
hair and beautiful complexions, and, six months later,
the host demanded heavy cheques,from his two guests,
who hadn't thought the ladies and the supper worth
while mentioning at home. They paid, of course, in the
interests of domestic peace and quiet. Both these stones
go to show how thoroughly woman lias got man under
her thumb.

place on 12th inst., and received the same inevitable

effect of utilising the pieces. Chumley welcomed the

folk, and one spirited youth recovered sufficient spirits to
hail another with “ Iluiloa, Cockroach!” By the way, the
Jerseys dodge the coming and speed the parting guest
very cleverly. On this occasion they entered the ball-
room at a quarter to ten, and supper—soon after which
most right-minded people go away—was put on at 11!
One hour and a quarter of social martyrdom is what the
Jerseys arrange for ; for there is always the foolish
maiden who stays till the hostess yawns to he reckoned
with.
I am quite pleased to see it advertised in a Sydney
daily that a “ young lady, who has passed Univ. exam.,
wants position of trust, shop or private family.” Because
it proves that every woman who surges up to the ’Varsity,
and indulges in a more or less successful tug of intellect
in merry March, doesn’t always discover that now she
knows B from a bull’s foot. Anything but dreary
scholasticism is “low.” Really, education is wanted all
over the house—from the coals to the cock-loft, and if
the ’Varsity girl is going to he sensible, and exercise her
mental faculties, just so as to be a brainy woman who
doesn’t boundary-fence her existence with arithmetic and
irritated infants just because she once cut a successful
figure on an examination list - well, the ’Varsity girl has
my sincerest wishes.
That was a curious advt. which “ two Tasmanian sisters”
inserted in a London paper recently—thanking the police
for their courteous attentions—curious in Australia at
least where policeman B 42 stands on the kerb with his
mouth open while old ladies fall helplessly out of cabs, or
dodge and double among the traffic. But I am told the
London policeman is quite a feature. He takes you on
his arm and guides you across streets as if you were a
poodle-bn a string ; he finds you almost before you are
lost and restores you to your relatives whether you have
any or not, and you may drop packages as if you were a
moulting Christmas-tree in the sure and certain hope
that lie will find every one of them. No wonder the
Tasmanian sisters brokenly blessed bobby on parting. I
hope they tipped him, too. The very best line of blessing
wants at least two-and-Six as a sinker.

Athenasum Hall Max O’Rell.
Friday last at Sydney Royal saw the final
performance of Pettitt and Buchanan’s eminently
virtuous combination of limelight and stage-
carpentry, “ The Trumpet Call,” and on Saturday
night was revived the good old New-Babylonesque
of “London Lay by Day,” which has, however
a deal of country in it, and not a little midnight!
The plot of the present production is crassly
simple, and as ancient as Lear in its abject com-
monplaceness. The inevitable girl who is im-
possibly virtuous, but who likewise appears to
nearly everybody but the impossibly good young
man who believes m her, to be proportionately
impossibly vicious, meanders through many
beautifully - painted acres of glorified scenery
to a triumphant recrudescence of her hypo-
thetical goodness at a late date, and all ends
happily ever afterwards. The good young
man whose faith in the maiden aforesaid is
built, like Mr. Gladstone’s, upon an immovable
rock, is a chirpy brand of “chappie’’who does a
deal of naughtiness with the air and unction of a
saint, and who, when not making insouciant love
under a dove-cote, is usually engaged in singing
pro-notes for a humorous Hebraic swindler who
ought to have made several fortunes as premier
clown of a high-class circus. Of course, there is
the villain whose English is fringed with French
but whose colloquial Anglo-Saxon is an excellent
article whenever he is in a hurry, whose clothes tit
like a glove, whose cigars appear at a distance to
be above all price, and whose wickedness is a
superfine line of goods with gilt edgings and many
varieties of seductiveness. The play is rich also in
a heavy father who is a general, and an American
who has lived 30 years in Chicago in a vain
endeavour to master an Irish brogue, a gardener
who drives a hansom and wins prize-fights be-
tween his fares, a lord who is billed also as a
“baronet,” a reformed solicitor whose villainy
breaks out occasionally between broad interstices
of much innate virtue, and many other people of
all sorts and conditions, who do their honest best
to add an attraction to scenery which is beyond
all praise. In all, 29 billed performers glide
between the glimpses of the footlights, assisted in
a vaguely generous way by many unbilled citizens,
policemen, Bohemians, and a number of other
individuals eloquently classed as “ etc.”
* * #
“London Day by Day” gives the ever-
Eland Holt a big opportunity to shine—and he
firmly grasps it by the fetlock. His Harry Ascalon
is a massive creation snatched from the gilded
spaces of an idealised Petticoat Lane, soaked in
the aurated atmosphere of Lombard-street, and
dried in the salubrious vapours of Seven Dials.
It riots in the morality of the pawnshop, and
savours hugely of the seamy financial aide of
Judaism. Asa matter of necessity, Mr. Charles
Glenny is a first favourite with a “scrumptious”
part_ like that of Frank Granville, and his love-
making is of an eminently taking variety of
foolishness, and all his ways are ways of plea-
santness, although most of his paths are anything
but peaceful. As the villain with a smile that
never grows cold, and a suavity of manner
worthy an executioner or a highwayman, Mr.
Albert Norman works his way steadily to stage
distinction w r ith wonderful success and as much
“ hissed ” applause as any martyr of ancient days
could consider himself entitled' to. Miss Kate
Bishop is the young, lovely and suffering maiden
whom the world thinks more badly of than at
present it does of Deeming, but who deserves as
much happiness as St. Elizabeth of Hungary, and
gets it in the last act. Miss Edith Blande is a
considerable success as the adventurous and daring
wife of the seductive villain with a French accent
in his sober moments, and Mr. E. B. Russell is a
cheerful solicitor who has become tired of unin-
terrupted degradation and yearns for another
trade-mark of experience. Mr. H. R. Roberts
as Tom Galloway, a boxing Jehu; Miss Vir-
ginia Vivienne las Dolly Blossom ; Miss Flora
Anstead as her “ ma,” Miss Ivy Scott
as Jack, and a lot of other performers in
many other characters helped along the
Stage-carpentry and gave the lime-light some-
thing to shine upon until a late hour, when the
glory broke up, and folk departed to catch the
last tram. The scenery is magnificent; houses
fall to pieces into country landscapes, and all the
various sets reflect etc,, on the artist, Mr. George
Gordon. Mrs. Bernard Beere succeeds Bland
Holt on Saturday next.

At Sydney Her Majesty’s, that lovely
vision of juvenile delight, “Jack Sheppard,” who 1
appears nightly until further notice in the
seductive part of Miss Billee Barlow, is piling the
Ossa of success on the Pelion of crowded houses,,
and all goes merrily as the clank of the gentle
Newgate Sheppard’s chains. The show unwinds-
itself with a fool-pad-like urbanity, and the
gallery is improving vastly in its rendition of
the chorus, “ Ri-tooral.” When the burlesque
has run another twelvemonth, the “ gods ’’ may be-
able to strike in within three bars behind the
mummers ; at present they come in with the
chorus about three minutes after the next verse-
has been under weigh. Mr. Harry Shine and Mr.
George Walton are brightening up considerably iw
the parts of Blueskin and Jonathan Wild re-
spectively, and Edgeworth Bess is not nearly so-
demonstrative in the alleged skirt-dance in the
second act. Altogether the show is jogging along
nicely, and everybody is as well as can be
expected.

The annual lawn-tennis tournament terminated
on Saturday, disastrously for N. S. Wales, who only
secured one out of the seven open matches played. The
one match won bv New South Wales, however, was a
“daisy,” it being the All Comers’ Singles, in the final
of which Webb of Sydney defeated Green of Melbourne.

male caste is a very strong one, and Messrs. Tither-

burst of applause which followed their strong scene
in the second act—the pathetic scene in which the
girl-wife of an hour is branded as adventuress,
traitress, and thief, and is compelled to boar the
stigma of the blame which should attach to the
beautiful and baneful Oomtesse Zicka—a genuine
adventuress with a plus against her brain and a
minus against her heart. Suitably clad in raiment
0 f shimmering satin she glides majestic through
the piece, doing all the mischief she can compass,
and making a bold attempt to revolutionise the
history of Europe within two hours. Inspired by
.a member of the Russian police, Baron Stein,
inspired also by her love for Captain Beauelere,
Zicka steals a tracing of the defences of Con-
stantinople, and manages to put all the opprobrium
upon the poor little English girl, who doesn’t
know enough to come in out of the diplomatic
rain ’
Mr. Titheradge as Henry Beauelere was
an image of delight to Saturday night’s audience,
as they watched the final triumph of diplomacy
over intrigue, and his quiet dry humour as he
“ trapped his mouse ” iu the form of the scared
but unrepentant Zicka was a lovely sight to
contemplate. Mr. Lyndal puts quite unexpected
force into the character of Captain Beauelere, who
is, after all, only a well-groomed, uninterest-
ing young man to whom any “ right-
thinking person ” would properly grudge the
charming and forgiving Dora, who, in the person
of Mrs. Molyneux, is as pretty as a picture
and as graceful as an antelope, and who, moreover,
certainly thoroughly deserves to triumph over her
rival, albeit in her tragic moments she is wanting
slightly in dramatic backbone. The Russian
police-agent, Baron Stein, who wanders about
entrapping women of fashion as paid spies, is ex-
pounded with dry irony by Mr. Boucicault, whose
make-up as an elderly gentleman
is almost one of the most com-
plete disguises he ever assumed ;
and Mr. Lawrence has seldom
done better than with his digni-
fied Count Orloff, that other
victim of Zicka’s treachery. Miss
Watt-Tanner, in her Marquise,
with her foreign accent, her allu-
sions to the mythical Don Alva,
and her devotional thankfulness
when her daughter wins that
pearl of great price (a husband)
gives a capital character sketch.
Miss Florence Seymour struggles
bravely with the repellant part
of the Countess Zicka, and makes
up for some lack of artistic
power in strength of artistic
costume. Miss Temple’s Lady
Henry is not quite fair to that
sparkling little “ Ambassadress ” ;
more chic and less monotony of
manner would be refreshing amid
so many harrowing scenes. Mr.
H. Hodson, Mr. George Hill, and
others, ably help out the piece in
several minor characters. “Diplo-
macy ” is capitally mounted, and
its programme is brimful of poetic
quotations hence it ought to
have a decently long season.

The fates fight against George Musgrove,
for the time being, and the new Gaiety Burlesque
season, at Melbourne Opera House, made a sick
start on Saturday. Marion Hood had broken
down in one of her ankles, or something, an
accident which not only left the company without
a good female voice to call its own, but necessi-
tated a change of parts amongst some of the
weaker vessels. Probably this misfortune was
largely accountable for the absence of fizz and
sparkle from an entertainment which is said to
have effervesced elsewhere. It is inconceivable
that even the Johnnies of dear old England could
find delight in “Faust Up to Date,” as performed
on Saturday. So in the joyful hope of the afore-
said Marion’s return to the rescue The Bulletin
lays aside in the meantime the cold-blooded
remarks inscribed upon its little slate. An im-
printed preliminary notice is subject to alterations
and repairs. Ta-ra-ra-Boom-der-ay
No apology is needed for a more than ever
delightful performance of “ Lucia ” at the Prin-
cess’s. The merits of Signora Cuttica and of all
the artists who participated in the musical
triumph of Saturday night were recognised with
greater gusts of enthusiasm than ever made the
Alexandra roof shake. The humorous side of
this delirium lay in the fact that many of the con-
gregation were evidently persuaded that they had
just discovered a new star and satellites, of whose
existence they refused to be convinced until a w ily
astronomer like J. Cassius Williamson had brought
them under notice. In truth, the gifted soprano
seemed to sing the sweeter, and go more con-
vincingly mad for the sake of her improved sur-
roundings, but only in a spectacular sense can the
second coming of the Italians be really more
worthy of admiration than their first appearance
in Donizetti’s opera. The four performances of
“ Lucia ” will terminate this (Thursday) evening,
and on Saturday they start with “La Traviata.”

Jenny Lee fills up the gaps between grand
opera with “ Jo,” an arrangement so grotesquely
original that it should advertise itself, and ensure
good houses for the little woman’s farewells. Any
jaded person in search of a iiew sensation can go
early to the Princess’, without consulting the
advertisements, and wait for the overture to
indicate whether Cuttica or Jenny Lee is on that
night. Miss Jenny will pop up in her immortal
character twice this week, Wednesday and I riday,
and three times next week.

MRS. BLAND HOLT.
“ Uncles and Aunts ” has thriven passing
well at Melbourne Bijou, where Mr. Tom Cannam,
in the capacity of a grizzly spectre who walks
abroad under the name of ’Kiah, is nightly
received with the applause due to a new variety
of joke. ’Kiah’s unearthly behaviour harmonises
with his weird, peculiar aspect,- and his tout
ensemble is well worth a visit. There are but
two more opportunities for gloating over ’Kiah,
however, because he will quit his old haunts after
Friday. On Saturday the B. and B. company,
reinforced by Sass and Cecil Ward, will try
“ Gloriana ” for the first time in Australia. This
comedy, adapted from the French by James
Mortimer, is not too naughty, but just naughty
enough, and the management expect it to have a
real good time.
Invigorated by the recollection of a full
house for “Romeo and Juliet ” the night before,
the Alexandra people broke out furiously on
Saturday in a robust entertainment called “ Dead
Beat.” A hasty glance at this five-act panorama
revealed some pleasing symptoms of originality on
the part of its London authors, and the plot seems
to soar above the average level. For one thing,
the persecuted hero is blind a good deal of the
time and has to go around feeling his unhappy
way with a stick. Finally, the green shade is
lifted from this young man’s eyes, and the chief
source of misery blows himself up with dynamite,
thus removing the last barrier that has separated
Lily Dampier from her love. Meanwhile, Dam-
pier maintains a striking position in the fore-
ground and makes matters lively for all concerned.
He is a humorous wrongdoer of a foreign turn of
mind, his main idea being the attachment of im-
portant papers, which he secretes inside the lining
of his hat with ulterior views. Sometimes he
retires to a pub. for a drink, and emerges again in
somebody else’s hat, so that ju t when he purposes
selling the papers at a big stage price they are
absorbing the perspiration of an unknown brow.
Dampier makes a mark in a character that is
quaint and quite refreshing. Athobvood, Miss
Julia Merton, Rolfe, Robert Vernon, and about
sixteen other people are mixed up in “Dead
Beat,” which received a loud and shrill reception
on Saturday.
* *
Widow O’Brien, at the Royal, is finishing
her Australian career with much vigour, and a full
house for Friday night, when Sheridan breathes
his last in the estimable character of Nora’s
mother, is bound to ensue. On Saturday (21st.)
the Bland Holt company, late of Sydney, will
come out almost as good as new, after the lapse
of weary years, in “A Sailor’s Knot.” Bland
Holt is generally regarded as the god of melodrama
in Melbourne, and his many admirers are eager
for the fray.
»* * *
The Melbourne halls are very much in
evidence for the present. Miss Adey Brunell,
attired in sweet Ophelian garb, gave her first
dramatic recital at the Athenaeum on Saturday,
and made a visible impression on a crowded
recherche audience. Miss Brunell’s style is chaste
and natural always—if she never quite touches
the sublime, she certainly never misses her mark
and becomes ridiculous. Anyone who can give
“In the Usual Way” and “Godiva” with such
nice effect as Miss A.B. produces, is entitled to
appear again from time to time. The show,
which lasted a modest two hours, was largely
aided by Benno Scherek, who extracted an occa-
sional scherzo from the inevitable piano.
* *
Max O’Rell is— vide R.'S. Smythe—“ the
Man of the Hour,” at tbe Athenaeum, since
Monday last. The hall doesn’t hold more than a
regiment of individuals at one time, and as there
is probably a small army waiting to sit at the feet
of the much-advertised French gentleman, he will
be pouring forth his modern philosophy for several
nights to come.

not the least appalling event which will be charged
against Deeming when he comes to settle up—

Toney girls and dudes galore—
House was crowded to the door
Waiting for the treat in store.
Though the curtain rose at eight.
Rose on “Faustus up to Dale,”
For that treat they had to wait
Till their ardour did abate—
Ta-ra-ra-Boom-der-ay,
Ta-ra-ra-Boom-de-rot,
Ta-ra-ra, -&c., &c.
Spite of all the puff and blow
Spent upon this gaudy show,
People found it rather slow,
Void of “snap ” and lacking “go.”
When at last tire curtain fell.
Few remained to shriek and .tell,
Mashers hurried out pell-mell.
Sad at heart, and saying, “ Well,
Ta-ra-ra-Boom-der-ay,” &c,
Lonnen, put in Leslie’s place,
Scarcely seemed to fill the space ;
Barren's dear, familiar face
Would be missed in any case.
Courtneidge fell extremely flat,
Though he had a lot of “fat,”
So it was no wonder that
On, his chest a damsel sat—
Ta-ra-ra-Boom-der-ay, &c.

Slowly waned the public grin.
As they watched this gilded sin,
Asking where the fun came in.
Where, O! where was fair Miss Hood ?
Absent, be it understood.
New girls did the best they could,
Not so bad, but not too good-

On the good old music-hall,

At Poverty Point,
A London critic says of an actress now in Australia,
that she is distinguished by “a connubial adaptability to
circumstances.” We have looked at this remarkable
statement all round, and we give it up.
Rosie Nott, of the new Gaiety Company, is the daughter
of Cicely Nott, a once popular singer and comedienne.
Lohr, returning from Adelaide with Carl Hertz &Co.,
found just time enough for one drink in Melbourne before
catching the Maoriland boat. Ho reported good business
in the Holy City with Musgrove’s variety combination,
the “gate” having closed on six hundred a week.
Berry, the British ex-hangman, proposes to go round
lecturing on “Men I Have Hung.” And now, in our
mind’s eye, we can see Berry opening negotiations with
the much-travelled Smythe for a lecturing tour round the
world, and we can hear Smythe’s shriek of rage when he
gets Berry’s letter, and we can faintly imagine Berry’s
howl of indignation when he gets Smythe’s reply.
The new Gaiety Co. orchestra includes good old La
Feuillade, fiddler for a quarter of a century, Phil. Lang-
dale, Cowen, bassoonist, Weston, senior, first violin, Ess,
clarionet—a tip-top local contingent. The band numbers
22, all told.

“ H’m ; that would not do in Australia, Mr. Cook: they’re

It was noticed that La Gighalton kissed J. C. Williamson
with less relish than she hugged Billy Elton with.
The now historic sale of Anson’s library in Sydney is
reminiscent of the great sale by Giltgore, the draper, of a
portentous quantity of stuff that came out of an imaginary
wreck. He kept the shop shutters up, a mysterious
attraction of itself, and inside you found the gas glaring
on the salvage, hung about with seaweed.
J. D. Marsh, who staged-raanaged the recent amateur
performance, at Sydney Garrick, in aid of the funds of
the 'Women’s College, is G. V. Brooke’s prompter of the
early sixties.

Dear Bulletin, —A worthy R.C. prelate left us last
week, after accumulating Peter’s Pence for many years
in our midst. On his departure we gave him a banquet -
slabs of beef, the übiquitous turkey, Irish wlvsky and
doubtful fizz. The captain of our local army (four privates
and three officers) occupied the chair, having previously
(by right of his friendship with the prelate) advertised
his “store-goods” on the back of the emblazoned invita-
tion cards. During the conviviality—while we were
waiting the response of some modest man to a toast —
the impatient crowd roared to a wealthy squatter to give
us “Bill Adams.” Hereupon the histrionically-untutored
chairman (weight, 18st.) slowly and ponderously rose,
and solemnly called on Mr. William Adams to respond
to (the waiting toast. What price Snazelle?— Manaro
Shepherd.
Marie Halton’s farewell hen. at Melbourne Princess’s
was not a densely-packed occasion by any means. Her
“ Dorothy ” performance knocked a hole in her prestige,
anyway, and perhaps “ La Cigale ” is too serious an opera
for a city that is bracing up its nerves for fresh taxation.
But when manager Williamson, after returning thanks on
Marie’s behalf, suggested that it would be many a long
day before the audience saw another “Grasshopper” of
her calibre, he evidently hit public opinion in the bull's-
eye.

The late Mr. Barcroft H. Boake was the
eldest son of the well-known Sydney photographer,
who, in the terrible affliction which has befallen
him, will have the sympathy of all.
In Memoriam—Barcroft Henry Boake.
Last Thursday appeared in Thk Bulletin soma
verses entitled “A Wayside Queen.” r ihe poem
in question was one only of many contributed by
Barcroft Henry Boake to the columns of this
paper; but “A Wayside Queen” is specially
interesting, from the fact that the day after its
appearance, the body of the gifted young author
was found by a wood-cutter hanging to a tree by
the lash of a stock-whip, at Folly Point, Long
Bay, North Shore —he had committed suicide .
Mr. Boake was by profession a surveyor; his age
was five-and-twenty. For a long period he had
been engaged in the service of Mr. Lipscomb,
district surveyor of Wagga, and his poems smell
of the eucalypts and breathe the fresh breath of
the bush. When his work in the south-west ended,
he came to seek some steady employment in Sydney
—his quest was a fruitless one. Quiet, reticent,
gentle-mannered almost to a fault, his ill-success
appears to have worried him unduly, and is sup-
posed to have induced insomnia—that torch-bearer
to the tomb for the man of too sensitive nerves.
Albeit, he disappeared from his home on the 2nd
of the current month —his body was found eleven
days after in a lonely copse that overlooked one
of the reaches of Sydney Harbour. Only the other
day, the first of modern French story-tellers, the
ablest pupil of the great Flaubert, the brilliant
and versatile Guy de Maupassant, successful man
of letters, strove to take his life, although his foot
had touched the topmost pinnacle of literary and
artistic triumph. Some century or so ago, the
gifted boy, Chatterton, poisoned himself in a
garret! Here are the antipodes ! The men who
glide hastily away to the Never IS ever Land do
so from different motives, and in different modes.
Some complete nearly the whole tramp of life with
heart unflinching, facing the drought and the heat
and the burden of the day with never so much as
a sigh. Mile upon mile the plodder covers till the
terrible life-weariness comes on him, perhaps only
at the final stage, and then he throws down his
“ swag” for ever, and makes a pillow of his burden
for a final rest. Others again attempt hardly a
day’s march of the life-journey. The plain
stretches out barren and sere before them, the
shimmering lakes appear as mirage, and the gold
glitters but as withered yellow grass beneath the
burning rays of a torrid sun—the prospect is
nothing but thankless labour and dreariness, so
with a sigh they surrender, and enjoy a surcease
of sorrow almost before tasting of its bitterness.
So was it with Barcroft Boake ! All who knew
him admired him as much for the innate gentle-
ness of his nature as for the talents which might
have one day blossomed into genius and conducted
their possessor to fame and fortune. In the
Christmas number of this paper appeared one of
the poor lad’s poems, which reads weirdly pro-
phetic in the light of the writers fate. It is
entitled
WHERE THE DEAD MEN LIE.
Out on the wastes of the “Never, Never, ’
That’s where the dead men lie,
There where the heat-waves dance for ever,
That’s where the dead men lie ;
That’s where the earth’s lov’d sons are keeping
Endless tryst—not the west wind sweeping
Feverish pinions, can wake their sleeping—
Out where the dead men lie.
Where brown Summer and Death have mated,
That’s where the dead men lie,
Loving with fiery lust unsated.
That’s where the dead men lie;
Out where the grinning skulls bleach while ly.
Under the saltbush sparkling brightly,
Out where the wild dogs chorus nightly,
That’s where the dead men lie.
Deep in the yellow, flowing river,
That’s where the dead men lie,
Under its banks where the shadows quiver.
That’s where the dead men lie;
While the platypus twists and doubles,
Leaving a train of tiny bubbles ;
Rid at last of their earthly troubles,
That’s where the dead men lie.
East and backward pale faces turning,
That’s how the dead men lie ;
Gaunt arms stretched with a voiceless yearning,
That’s how the dead men lie.
Oft in the fragrant hush of nooning.
Hearing again their mother’s crooning,
Wrapt for aye in a dreamful swooning,
That’s how the dead men lie.
Nought but the hand of night can free them *
That’s when the dead men fly ;
Only the frightened cattle see them —
See the dead men go by ;
Cloven hoofs beating out one measure,
Ridding the stockman know no leisure,
That’s where the dead men take their pleasure,
That’s when the dead men fly.
Ask too, the never-sleeping drover,
He sees the dead pass by,
Hearing them call to their friends—the plover,
Hearing the dead men cry.
Seeing their faces stealing, stealing,
Hearing their laughter wealing, pealing,
Watching their grey forms wheeling, wheeling
Round where the cattle lie.
Strangled by thirst and fierce privation.
That’s how the dead men die
Out on the farthest-stretching station,
That’s how the dead men die ;
Hardfaced greybeards, youngsters callow,
Some mounds cared for, others fallow,
Some deep down, yet others shallow,
Some having but the sky.

stated Total loan liability, .£20,509,000; interest

ment proposes to amend the laws relating to
medical practitioners; likewise those relating to
general cemeteries.

Two-thirds of Britain’s food-supply now
comes from abroad, and the quantity is increasing
every year. The Foreign-trade party quotes this
as an evidence of the increased purchasing power
of the country. Another way of putting it is that
only one-third of Britain’s food is grown in the
country, and the quantity is diminishing annually,
because production is steadily going to perdition,
and has been going there ever since Freetrade
began. It all depends on which way you look
at it.
