A, few weeks ago it was cabled that “the
works of the Norway Steel Company, at Boston
(U.S.) have been closed owing to the taxation on
raw material used m the production of steel ”
1 Ins news came, not from America, but from the
usual I reetrade sources in London, and was the
subject of a great wild whoop in the Australian
shoddy press. It now transpires, according to
news per Insco steamer, that not one, but several
iron and steel works contemplated closing when
the mail left, not owing to “taxation of raw
material, which is bosh, the raw material being
locally supplied, but owing to over-production and
a consequent glut in the market. Therefore,
pyclney 1). 1. and the Evening News are requested
in common honesty, to withdraw their frenzied
leaders, and explain how matters really stand
1 homas Bent is the new Speaker of
the Victorian Assembly. There were three
candidates Duffy, Wrixon, and Bent, and Bent
got in because, being the most objectionable of the
three, his name was put last. Ifirst of all Duffy
was put up, and lost because the friends of
VVrixon and Bent voted against him. Then
Wrixon went to the poll, and Bent’s friends voted
against him because they wanted Bent to get in,
and Duffy’s friends voted against him because
they were mad at him for spoiling Duffy’s
chances, so he was also rejected. Then there was
only one candidate left, and Bent got in without
t any division at all—to the disgust of nearly every-
body.
The Ananias of the Cairns (Q.) Post
announces that “a petition in favour of the ex-
tension of Polynesian immigration has been
unanimously signed by the working-men of
Townsville.” “ Unanimously ”is good, consider-
ing the solid white labour vote which was cast at
Townsville a very few months ago. There is a
great sameness about petitions that are got up by
the moneyed classes. The red-nosed canvasser is
paid by results, and lie puts down signatures him-
self in the most reckless manner. The petitions
are inspected by the local bosses, and the in-
dividual who refuses to sign is very apt to be
‘’sacked.” Women, infants, dead men, loafers,
and people who never lived, all figure in the lists!
Ihe canvassers all sign their own petitions and
each others petitions, and in a town of GOO in
Habitants, you can always get 1000 signatures in
favour of any movement, even though it wouldn’t
tmcl two genuine supporters at the ballot box.

The W.A. hangman had a hard fio-ht
recently with four niggers whom he was trying to■
scrag officially in a lonesome place “up North ”
There was no gallows for one tiling, no qualified!
assistant, no trap-door, and none of the eon
venienees of a respectable, civilised hanging. The
performance had to bo done out in trie open
beneath a spreading tree, and the four were
placed on a dray, while GO or 70 other niggers
looked on in excited anticipation. Then the noose
was fixed round each man’s neck and hitched
on to a strong branch overhead, and the order
was given to move the dray away and let the law
take its course. Just here the trouble began. By
some unaccountable oversight the prisoners’ arms
had not been tied, and suddenly awaking to a
knowledge of the situation the four gave a horrible
yell and each grabbed the rope overhead with one
hand and field on like grim death to the dray with
the other. The horse, as it happened, was a very
old and feeble animal, and with the four aborigines
clutching the cart it couldn’t make any progress
It got an inch or two forward every now aiid°then
but it was always dragged back again, and despite
the most vigorous and vociferous walloping the
hanging didn’t progress worth a cent. Then the
hangman and his minion put their shoulders to
the wheel and shoved like the demons they were
and with a despairing yell from the four
the vehicle went away. One of the
party hung peacefully at the end of his
rope. Another hung and kicked furiously
ihe remaining two seized their ropes and pro-
ceeded to shin up them into the tree, and had to
be hauled down again. One of them, being hauled
down, gave up the struggle and died without
further argument, but the other woke up again
just when everybody began to think he was dead
and started to claw his way up the rope a second
time. Then he apparently thought better of it
and, letting go, became down with a thud, and
all was over. The aboriginal audience was much
amused, but the hangman went home sick, and
has never been the same executioner since.
Remarkable Incident.
A LECTURER DISCOVERS HIS DANGER AND LOSES
HIS LIFE—A TERRIBLE WARNING TO OTHERS.
Ii is a scent; in the lecture-room of a medical
college m New Orleans. The professor is
lecturing before an intelligent class of medical
students. He is describing the human body, its
defects, and the danger by which it is surrounded.
In order to illustrate it he has fluids from the
human body, which he is subjecting to chemical
tests.
Gentlemen, ’• he said, “Ihave described to
you the appearance of the human fluid in a
diseased state; I will now show you how the
same fluid appears in a healthy state,” and lie
subjected his own to the test. As he held it up to
the light for a moment, his hand trembled, he
caught his breath, he paled, and exclaimed.
Gentlemen, I have just made a most horrible
discovery; I myself have Bright’s disease of the
kidneys.
In less than one year he was dead.
The above dramatic and strictly true incident
shows the terrible danger and mysterious nature
of this modern disease, which may well be called
the demon of the present century. It steals into
the system like a thief, manifests its presence by
the commonest symptoms and fastens itself upon
the system before the victim is aware. It is
nearly as hereditary as consumption, quite as
common and fully as fatal. Entire families, in-
heriting it from their ancestors, have died, yet
none of the number knew or realised the
mysterious power which was removing them.
Hundreds of people die daily by what is called
heart disease, apoplexy, paralysis, spinal com-
plaint, rheumatism, pneumonia, and other com-
mon diseases, when in reality it is Bright’s disease
of the kidneys. ’These are solemn facts, but all
the more serious because of their solemnity:
r l here has never been but one remedy known for
the (*ure of Bright s disease, or even for its relief,
and that remedy has become the most popular of
any preparation known (o the world. It is
\\ arner s Safe Cure. It has taken men, and
women, too, who were in the lowest stages and
restored them to perfect health and strength. It
will invariably check the first stages of this
terrible disease, if taken in time. It is endorsed
by physicians, approved by scientists, and used by
the best people in the land. It is a scientific
preparation and owes its popularity wholly to its
power. It can be procured of any reliable dealer,
but great care should be exercised to secure the
genuine and not permit any unscrupulous dealer
to sell anything in its place.

Octogenarian Lord Bramwell’s death is
cabled. The most noteworthy thing he did of
late was writing a pamphlet on “ Drink,” opposing
the teetotallers, and urging that they never had
produced one great man. There was depth in it,
too, when he traced drinking customs to a lack of
true social feeling among the community.
Gladstone attended publisher John Mur-
ray’s funeral the other day. In his Murray-pub-
lished “Church and State,” which Macaulay
scarified, Gladstone insisted that only members of
the Church of England should have any share in
the Government. Macaulay said —“ The world
will carry even Mr. Gladstone round, in spite of
himself.”

lawyer, and his sister died on the same evening
Within a few minutes of each other, both victims
to brain-softening, and both suddenly. The mes-
senger taking the news of the sister’s death arrived
as the brother was breathing his last, behaving
been taken ill only half-an-hour earlier.

The late Earl of Huntingdon, father of Sir
Salmon Wilson’s prospective son-in-law, was not
only poor. He was not a favourite at Court be-
cause of the allegation that it was he who led
Albert Edward into that ancient scrape with the
Irish girl known as “Mrs. Albert.” Wales, at
the time, was quite as old and experienced as
Huntingdon, but the poor Earl got blamed tor
it all.

phonographess, and took down verbatim a prigged
sermon by the other, with the strangely-appro-
priate title of “ Mutual Help,” and then there was
a ruction.

Mrs. Humphrey Ward’s latest work—
“ David Grieve"—is full of reminders of “David
Copperfield.” Por instance, its hero is Davy,
and its heroine “Dora.” This latter is the
‘ 4 Agnes ” of Dickens’ work, while Mrs. Ward s
“Lucy” is its “Dora.” There is, too, a
inland “ Peggotty,” who employs the same ‘1
thank ’ee kindly, Davy.” It is also very evident
that the character of “ Elsie ” is largely borrowed
from the journal of Marie Bashkirtseff.

great necessity of the case is that the judge who

Newnes, M.P., of Tit-Bits notoriety, was
fairly “on his uppers” when he commenced his
inner The writer knew him well then, and has
often heard him tell how he struck oil. As a boy
he had been in the habit of clipping interesting
pieces from the newspapers, and at this time ho
had about a dozen large scrap-books full, bo as
a last resource, he tried to sell them to some of the
so-called comic papers. He vainly tramped Lon-
don streets for a whole, week, but could do noth-
ing with them, until at last he met a publisher
who remarked that he was willing to go halves
■with him in bringing out the scraps m a weekly
periodical. Newnes' next move was characteristic ;
‘‘ If it will pay,” he thought, “ I’ll try and manage
it myself.” And with the help of a couple of
hopeful friends he did. The idea took ; and that s
how the world's organised plagiarism vyas stalled.

The Cause of the Trouble.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Once upon a time, the Queensland Government
became suddenly afflicted with a pious sense of
horror at the treatment of the natives, and, in
consequence, issued nothing but blank cartridges
to the native police; and called upon the other
provinces to see how good they were. If there
was any “dispersing” of blacks to be done the
officer in charge found the bullets, or obtained
them from the stations, so that the niggers “got
there all the same ” in the end.
Many Sydney firms owned stations then, and
some of the partners, leading lights of religious
societies, who openly approved of the action of
the Queensland authorities, sent up ball-cartridge
amongst the station stores, but never asked what
became of it. The manager never reported that
the blacks were bad when he wrote down. He
was too wise.
One day Donovan, the super, of a large coastal
cattle-station owned by a very pious Sydney man,
since gone to his reward, came upon a camp
where the blacks had been having a great feed of
beef. As they had “given him fits” for some
time and kept him going from one end of the run
to the other, he did not feel in the pleasantest of
tempers as he surveyed the relies of the feast.
“Blessed if I won’t let the police take a turn
this time,” he muttered, and returned home to
send a messenger to the barracks.
The run was notoriously a bad one for blacks ;
there was a deen fringe of mangrove swamps, in
addition to some broken, scrubby ranges where
they could always find refuge. The police hap-
pened not to be on patrol, and turned up that
evening.
“ How are you off for cartridges?” was the
first question of the officer’s, after the horses were
turned out.
“ Devilish short; hanged if I think we have got
Examination proved that they had scarcely a
couple of rounds. This was embarrassing. The
next station was thirty miles away, and at the
telegraph station at the crossing, some ten miles
off, it was very unlikely that they would have any.
However, the storekeeper was despatched there
on spec., with instructions to get back some time
that night. , , , ~
Now, unfortunately, there was a pub. at tne
crossing, and the storekeeper was a man of great
thirst; but as Donovan was hard pushed he
chanced it, and entrusted a discreetly-worded
telegram to Sir Yahoo Geebung, C.M.G., request-
ing him not to forget “ the usual sporting material
iiAhe next consignment of stores/’
Sir Geebung sat in his private office the next
morning just at the time the natives < n his
northern station were suffering retribution tor
their depraved appetites, when his telegrams, &c.,
were brought to him by his head clerk. Some ot
them had been opened in the course of business,
and amongst those opened was one that ran as
follows. ~.. ,
“ Hurry uplotsof cartridges niggers killed all
yom- cattle and we must wipe them out, or we 11 wipe
Here followed a signature that looked like
“ Blocdandouns.”
And this had been read by the whole ot the
decorous office down to the office-boy '■
The wretched storekeeper had got “tight, lost
Donovan’s telegram, and sent one of his own
written “under the influence.” The operators
bad eliminated the frequent adjectives and turned
“they’ll ” into “we’ll,’ that was all.
In the afternoon came another wire with the
orthodox signature of “J- G. Donovan
appended : , . , , ,
“First telegram dispatched ; send her drunk,
an Si?Geebung had a fit, then locked his door and
went to Ids private bottle. Poor Donovan, m
hopes of retrieving, had sent a short wire : ‘ ' irst
telegram despatched, sender drunk. \V ill ex-
plain.” And the operator, who had made a night
of it with the storekeeper, had mixed it up
supposed to have been the real cause of
the Government sending up ball-cartridge again
instead of blank.

They didn’t like to hang Deeming on
Queen’s Birthday.
The original Ingram (of London Illus-
trated News) made his fortune (before iris news-
paper) out of Parr’s Life Pills.
Carlyle to Dully: “If you get as much
gold as will make a bridge across from Australia
to England, it won’t be worth a mealy potato.”
The Melbourne Customs department has
a suspicion that Chinese crackers are under-
invoiced, and so is taking the imports at invoice
price, as (he law allows. This comes just before
Queen’s Birthday, and the importers are in con-
sternation.
In London one woman in every sixty is a
confirmed gin-drinker, one in every twenty is a
pauper, and one in every thirteen is unable to read
or write. And it is the bony infants of this
unwholesome trio who are supposed to raise their
voices and thank Heaven in a joyous chorus
because they are jointly and individually “ a happy
English child.”
Among the curious documents preserved
in the British Museum is a letter from the Duchess
of Marlborough’s secretary to the poet Pope, which
reads thus :—“ Sir, —My lady, the Duchess of
Marlborough, being drunk, was unable to see you
when you called yesterday.” Good, old, truthful
female. Your duchess of to-day gets drunk occa-
sionally just the same, and then she adds lying to
her other sins by saying that she has influenza.
Dear Bulletin,— Your paragraph re th e
storm-destroyed Victorian kirk recalls a some-
what similar Quirindi (N.S.W.) incident of last
year. They were erecting a weatherboard church
and the builder, running short of timber, put the
roof on before the sides were up. A heavy wind-
storm, which left a track of uprooted trees for
over sixty miles, came along and demolished it.
It also bodily lifted from its supports the Castle
Mountain Church (Scotch), distant three miles,
belonging to the same district, and erected
same builder, a number of years previously. The
“ unco good,” on the afternoon of the occurrence,
were holding a bazaar in aid of the building fund,
and “ gaffing ” was indulged in! Was it a judg-
ment ?— Wheatsheaf.
Up to date, Australian
mines have yielded 295
millions sterling worth of
gold.
Among the recent arri-
vals at Geraldton, W.A.,
were some half-dozen Japan-
ese “ladies.” What with
Chinese pirates, yum-yura
maidens, coolies and abori-
ginal blacks, the Geraldton
district is becoming a perfect
Babel, and only needs the
proteges of Baron Hirsch to
settle on the grant of Gas-
eoyne-Ashburton land,which
was offered them, to com-
plete it.
The rapid mental decay
of Hinglish civilisation is
further indicated by the gib-
berings of a certain White
Rose League, composed of
fin de si'ecle Jacobites who
have discovered a “ right-
ful Queen ” in the Princess
Mary of Bavaria. Once a
month, or so, the polite
faction aforesaid meets to-
gether in somebody’s draw-
ing-room and swears alle-
giance to the Bavarian
female, after which a mys-
terious - looking flunkey
brings round the coffee and
they all drink to a new line
of Sovereigns. Maiy, who
is no longer a chicken, has
married into a royal family
of confirmed lunatics, and
the probability that her next
of kin would want to eat the
sceptre of Great Britain and
Ireland, if he ever got hold
of it, forms the only bond
of union between the White
Rose push and progressive
Radicalism. Otherwise there
appears no reason why this
little clique of used-up “con-
spirators ” shouldn’t be exe-
cuted on Tower Hill and
have their estates confiscated
by the Crown.

PROTECTION FROM COLD.-Proper
warmth is necessary for the preservation of
health for young and old.- By sending an order to
Finney, Isles and Co., Brisbane, with a remittance
to cover amount, a pair of excellent, soft, white
Blankets will be sent by return parcel post to any
address in the colony. Fine large size for single
beds, with postage included, 12s. 3d.; extra
quality, 15s. 3d. per pair; superior large, for
three quarter beds, 15s. l)d. and 18s. Od. ; super-
fine, for double beds, IDs. Gd. and 245. ; extra
super., largest size for double beds, with plenty to
tuck in all round, 235. Gd., 30s. and 355. ; superior
quality, state bed size, 40s. 50s. and 00s. per pair.
Residents in the country, by comparing the fine,
heat-giving quality and value of Finney, Isles and
Co.’s Blankets with the poor quality usually sold
at the same or higher prices, will find the result to
be as astonishing as satisfactory. Poor quality
will be easily detected by the raw, unfinished sur-
face of the inside. Customers living outside the
radius of the Parcel Post delivery should avail
themselves of it to the nearest point it reaches,
where they could send for their parcels.
Society
Not long ago, a N.S.W. surveyor asked his
district-surveyor for an increase in salary.
Reason assigned ; “ Hardship consequent on the
dearth of female society in the district.”
# * *
The Young Victorian Patriotic League,
which still exists owing to the nauseous jam
bestowed upon it by the “ respectable ” journals
of Melbourne, lias established a branch called the
Second-Class League, to induce dudes, counter-
jumpers, and “sich,” to defeat the Government’s
policy of railway retrenchment by persistently
travelling second-class. Why not in the dog-box
with the other pups ?
* * *
The Marshall Lyle and Reverend Gaol-
chaplain Scott’s little tiff about Deeming might be
summed up in a few lines by the pietist:—
“The journals have arrived.” Legend
outside the book-shop once a month. And when
the Australian proletarian goes home he finds his
steak a cinder, and the potatoes stones, while the
Missus, with the baby choking, face-downwards,
over her left arm, clutches in her right fist the
yellow-covered miser-murderer. •

* # #
Thus are “ choice ” minor N.S.W. Govern-
ment billets disposed of : The messengership in a
big department being vacant, many highly-snit-
able and well-recommended men applied. Gov.
Carington, however (this was immediately before
bis departure), waited upon the Minister and
claimed the billet for a “ friend” of his. “Cer-
tainly, your Excellency,” said the Minister;
“ Who is your friend ?” “My footman,” said the
yawning Governor, and the footman got it and
holds it. Again a messengership in another large
department became vacant, when a well-known
figure in political life got the billet for his coach-
man. It has for many years past been the prac-
tice for Governors to provide good Government
billets for their flunkeys at the expense of men
entitled to promotion. This system should be
knocked on the head.

Dear Bulletin, —Your version of the terra
“Jimmy Woodser ” may be correct in some parts
of Australia; but in three provinces, at least, a
solitary drink is always known as a “ Jack
Smithers.” However, it does not much matter,
for you get there all the same.—D.

In a Pitt-street “ Johnny-All-Sorts ” shop
there is now exposed for sale most of the stock-
in-trade (two brass-plates) of two recently-
exploded Sydney “banking” institutions. The
rest of the brass on which one of these “ banks "
traded is now in gaol.

The professional head of a Bananaland
Government department, domestically connected
with a prominent Minister, has been admitted to
Woogaroo Asylum. Immediate cause—the prick-
ing of the Mount Morgan gold-bubble.
* * •
A sensational assault and false-imprison-
ment case will shortly be heard by N.S.W.
Supreme Court. Plaintiff, a Police-Magistrate,
who claims £2OOO and has retained Mr. Want,
Q.C., alleges that defendant, dissatisfied with an
official decision given by him, got him into a
room, locked the door, and seriously assaulted him.

• # *
N.S.W. Chief Judge Owen’s decision in
favour of the defendants in the ease, Batt and
Pnrves v. Onslow and others, may be considered
as a complete exoneration of Mr. Robert Smith
(late Crown Solicitor) from the serious charge of
duplicity, brought against him by implication.
Messrs. Batt and Pnrves, in effect, charged
Mr. Smith, who was their solicitor, with
having suppressed a cablegram received from
Mrs. Onslow’s agent which resulted in their
paying .£12,000 more for Darling Island
than Mrs, Onslow was willing to accept.
Judge Owen was particularly severe, in
his judgment, against plaintiffs, who, instead of
getting back their £12,000, will probably have to
pay costs to the tune of £4OOO. Of course, the
case goes to the Full Court, and thence to the
Privy Council. By the time it is finished, the
costs will probably be £12,000.

Dear Bulletin, —Bartholomew, whose
death you recently noticed, did not drive the
ooacb by being thrown from which Lady Mary
Fitzroy was killed. It was Sir Charles Fitzroy
himself who drove the four-in-hand, and he was
the only one who escaped : Masters, the aide, had
his skull broken, and was trepanned, and lived
but a day or two ; Lady Mary was killed on the
spot. The horses bolted from Government-House
door, leaving the coachman and footman behind,
and some people said —but no matter. The writer
saw the whole thing from the play-ground of the
King’s School.—M.R.A. [A lot of other people
who saw the event are prepared to swear that Sir
Charles Fitzroy saw the whole proceeding from
the window. But it doesn’t matter. History is
built that way.—Ed. B.]
* * *
The same jealous curiosity that prompts a
leading actor to go and see a rival play an
important character is apparent in the big-wigs
of the Bench. Judge Molesworth stood right
under Hodges during the latter end of the Deem-
ing trial, and walked out of Court just as the
jury retired. Having seen (or heard) how his
brother actor shaped in a strong tragic role,
Molosworth took not the least interest in the
final spectacular act, and was apparently the only
member of the audience who didn’t wait to see the
curtain fall. Enough for him that Hodges hadn’t
made a great hit.
The proprietress of a certain establishment
of which much mention was made in a recent
ugly down-south seduction case is nearly related
to a local family that is very “swagger ” indeed.
The beastly skeleton nearly rattled out of the
cupboard several times during the trial.
# * *
Premier Shiels thinks that his colt, Duffy,
was poisoned before the race for the Victorian
Speakership. L. L. Smith, the Government
doctor, thinks it was only a decayed tooth and a
sore throat. [N.B. —This has been cabled to
England as a quid for the Duke of Westminster’s
quo.]
* • •
All the holy cranks of Australia arc
writing letters to Deeming in his last hours. One
pious female has sent him a small bag fall of
earth which, she asserts, was dug up on Mount
Zion, also a long letter of exhortation, a pamphlet,
and some dried flowers which, it seems, also came
from Zion. Considering that what used to bo
Zion is now a species of ash-heap infested by half
the goats in Palestine, and no respectable female
could go there without risk of breaking her neck or
of being knocked down and slobbered on by a
camel, there is an air of discrepancy about the
flowers, but no doubt the old lady believes in them.
Another woman has sent Deeming a letter of 1G
pages exhorting him to “believe,” and offering to
put up a monument to him (on condition that he
leaves her his autobiography); and several more
have forwarded Bibles with passages marked which
seem likely to interest a man who is just going to
be banged. Almost all the Bibles have flowers in
them. Some anonymous benefactor lias forwarded
a quantity of jam in pots, and among the other
things which have come to hand are some
slippers, two of Spurgeon’s sermons, one
prayer-book, and a bound volume of some
pious magazine or other. All these contributions,
however, have been blocked at the gate, and the
only comforting document which has reached the
prisoner is the famous tailor’s bill from Sydney.
# * *
The vice-regal household in Melbourne is
said to have been grieved and shocked by The
Bulletin picture of Lady Hopetoun pushing a
perambulator. If the Countess wishes it to be
distinctly understood that she wouldn’t wheel her
own “pram.” for worlds, friends will please accept
this intimation.
Two ladies from the bush visited a Bris-
bane orphanage, under cover of acomniittee-man’s
card of introduction. The matron was affable but
a trifle “sudden.” She took the visitors into one
apartment and comprehensively explained • “ This
is one room. The rest are each like this. And
that passage will take you the way out by the
back-door. Good afternoon, and thanks.”
# * *
The girl patrons of Victorian football are
evidently most partial to the decollette players,
savages who sport large arms bare to the shoulders
and make a seductive display of naked neck and
legs. As matches grow more fleshly every season
the number of maidens who bring opera-glasses to’
bear upon the struggle increases apace. The
darlings experience, in their little way, all the
delights of a bald head watching the ballet.
* # *
The Victorian Artists’ Society open their
new picture-gallery at Eastern Hill, Melbourne,
on May 28th. Gov. Hopetoun has been captured
for an inaugural dinner and other ceremonies, and
it is to be hoped that a vigorous attempt to revive
an almost moribund interest in a local industry
will not be unsuccessful. The “patrons ”of art
are not hungry for pictures these bad times, but
they might take a few chances in raffles if II.E.
will set the example. Or if the president can lure.
His Ex. into gammoning to buy several of the
“ cronkest ” works at abnormal prices, a wild rush
for the others might result.
# *
“ The whole course of European history
in the Napoleonic period,” says a London journa-
list, “ was changed by the accident of an obscure
Corsican attorney of feeble health marrying a
woman as unsoundly constituted as himself, and
having an epileptic son with a mania for war.”
Which reminds us, the biggest newspaper boom in
Australian history was caused by the accident of
an obscure Lancashire lunatic marrying a woman
of crazy, religious opinions, and having an
epileptic son with a mania for burying wives
under the hearthstone.
* * *
A taste for unorthodox marriages must
run in the feeble bacteria of the Stamford gore.
The racing earl whose death, without issue, let in
his cousin, the Cape of Good Hope parson (whose
son, the half-nigger, is now the earl), took two
very gay and festive women to the altar. The
first was an Oxford girl of the town, picked up in
his early university days. She withered like a
weed, and when it transpired that Stamford had
cast his dull, dreamy eyes upon a rather too cele-
brated London beauty, the Queen (runs the story)
wrote to the noble widower, asking him to pause
and consider before forming a second mesalliance.
To which appeal Stamford replied that, if neces-
sary, he intended to choose a third, fourth, or
fifth countess of any sort he liked. He suited
himself, and always seemed satisfied with his lot.
Sassiety used to ignore my Lady Stamford at Ascot
and other fashionable gatherings, but the hand-
some, stately one played her part to the supreme
satisfaction of her lawful owner, anyhow. At
home, as lady of the mam r, she won for herself
a good name, which didn’t change for the worse
after she became a rich widow,
* # *
Deeming’s alleged revelation to Miss
Rounsevell concerning a certain spot in a South
African cemetery, where he claims to have planted
an enormous sum in gold, is evidently his own
humorous way of trying to get square with the
girl that he has got to leave behind him. If lie
can only persuade the Rounsevell family that
they have got exclusive information about an
enormous pile of specie, then the gold-thirst will
presently come upon that misguided household,
and they will sell all their property to pay their
joint passages to the Cape, and will quarrel all
the way about thedivision oftheplunder, and, when
they get on land, each one of them will try either to
dodge or to assassinate the rest and get to the
place first. Then, when they do get there, they
will buy spades and pickaxes and dark-lanterns,
and lie around watching the spot, and waiting for
a murky night so that they can dig up the colli
unobserved. And at last, when the night comes,
and it is raining heavily, and the wind is flapping
drearily among the dripping trees, they will get
to work, with about six women shivering in the
slush and holding the lantern, and eight men—for
all the Rounsevill family and its branches and
collateral relatives will be there—digging savagely
and hungrily among the damp bones of the
dead generations, and they will excavate
an enormous hole and find nothing. Then
some of them will become idiotic with the
sudden shock, and be found next day gibbering
The shepherd sought to save a man,
And on to Zion light him,
But Satan saw and stormed, and sent
His son along to spite him.
TO BABY'S FELLOW-COUNTRYMEN.
Good people, now with one accord
Send praises up to Heaven ;
Your leading Lady to her Lord
A precious pledge has given ;
For him alone it was not meant,
Although he jubilates.
The country on t hat choice event
Itself congratulates.
This is no democratic girl,
Too delicate to thrive—
The babe’s a hoy and (what is more)
A strong Conservative,
A darling with a lofty sneer
For him that digs and delves;
So, whilst you compliment the peer,
Congratulate yourselves.
The ways of Providence are mixed,
And goodness only knows
The reason why your hopes are fixed
On “ teeny toddletoes.”
But should your hearts incline to grief
When taxes new annoy,
Throw up your hats, and find relief
In thinking of the boy.
When neighbour Jones looks In to say
His wife has got a son
Your gratitude you don’t display
For all that he has done.
But, should Lord Hopetoun feel inclined
To say as much to you,
Exclaim at once “ How very kind !
Congratulate us, do.”
TO THE SPEAKER OF THE VICTORIAN ASSEMBLY,
They have voted you into the chair, Tom Bent;
One day we shall call you a knight;
Now let your head stick to its hair, Tom Bent,
And try to look ever polite.
Restrain the warm members with gentle rebukes—
You mustn’t invite ’em to “ put up their docks,”
Nor throw down your wig on the floor, gadsooks !
And bid ’em come on for a fight.
You must purchase Mat. Davies’s gown, Tom Bent,
That garment embroidered in gold,
Which cost nearly two ’under’d poun’, Tom Bent,
When Matthew in affluence rolled.
Disguised in the glories of Speaker at last,
As chief among aft the good “ commoners” class’t,
We hope you’ll have done with the days that are past.
And not play the Tommy of old.
Your Hinglish is not very chaste, Tom Bent,
Your accent is not very sweet ;
For lamin’ you have a distaste, Tom Bent —
Vet O ! you’re a good ’tin to eat :
So, if you give dinners and entertain well,
And chuck in some French, such as pastry lev gel,
You can pansy yourself as no end of a swell,
And dazzle the bloomin’ elect.
Then ’ere’s to your ’elth with a song, Tom Bent,
In truth you’re a wonderful man,
And may you live ’appy and long, Tom Bent!
Though life is at best hut a span.
You ought to do well in that billet unique
To which you were somehow appointed last week,
For the duty of Speakers, thank God ! is to speak
As little as ever they can.
THE LOVE OF A “GOD.”
[For Tick Bulletin.]
She stood with the tall, painted turrets above her,
While I lingered and worshipped the boards where sh
trod—
From the rose in her hair to her instep I love her,
But what does she eare tor the love of a “ god V ”
Ah, belle of the stage ! if the gods should forsake you
Your bright star would fall like a stone from the sky •
You know ’tis the cheers of their godships that make con
And yet you begrudge them a blink from your eye."
While we sit in the darkness, and pay' you our duty,
You give not e’en that which our worship demands
You have eyes for the dress-circle swell and the beauty
Who think ’twould he “ vulgah ” to clap their vi,iu
hands.
Yet, we have romances, and we have our trouble
'Tis only the stage of our lives is so wide.
M.v Queen ! I’m as worthy' as any ga.y noble
Who strutted thro’ Home with a sword at his side’
Yet, bless her ! God bless her ! our fair prima donna
A lily, a daisy, a willow, a rose* !
And brightly and long may the lime-light shine on her
As belle of the ballet wherever she goes.
From.the crown of her bend to her instep I love her
With love that shall rest only tinder the sod
For, search t hro’ I he world and you’ll never discover
A queen of (lie footlights in love with a “ god."
H. L.

* # #
The Leeds (Eng.) Times heaves this moral
reflection after the retreating figure of Mrs.
Osborne, the pearl-thief, late of Holloway gaol :
From the period of Mrs. Osborne’s disappearance from
the country to the day when she returned to take her
trial for perjury, there was a sickly display of washed-out
sentiment in the namby-pamby columns of the namby-
pamby press, and since the aristocratic lady has been
waiting events in Holloway, ttie same sentimental clap-
trap has oozed out again, it goes without saying that if
Mrs. Osborne had been a decrepit old woman on crutches,
who earned tier living by hawking pins, and had been
captured by a posse of disguised policemen while in the
act of stealing an apron full of stale bread from the
chickens in the backyard, the namby-pamby press
aforesaid would have let her get six months without
going into hysterics about it, or pinching itself to raise
the pitying tear ; but as Mrs. Osborne happens to be an
aristocratic person with refined tastes and a pretty face,
it must needs shriek because her cell in Holloway hasn’t
been made into a drawing-room, and wall-bells "haven’t
been furnished for her to ring when she requires the
cook, or the housemaid, or the butler, or some other
menial.

Honourable Pat. Nugent, charged with
criminal assault in a railway-carriage, was, it is
cabled, let down more easily than Valentine
Baker. The gallant colonel, like the lion. Patrick,
was found guilty of a “common” assault only,
inasmuch as ho stopped short of gross brutality
when he found himself out in bis calculations, but
the court gave him a year and fined him £5OO. The
six months’sentence passed on Nugent looks like
a concession to his family connections.
On a notice-board outside West Melbourne
police station last week, there were six notices of
rewards, and five were for the conviction of in-
fanticides. In Melbourne dailies every day, there
is half-a-column of thinly-veiled advertisements,
offering to procure abortion.

A company of four itinerant Italian
musicians, well-known about Sydney, intend going
home shortly to see their families, whom they left
in Italy. It is said that these “ buskers,” for an
hour’s performance nightly at the Hotel Australia,
receive £250 a year, and that their takings average
£l2 a week each.

Some queer stories, more or less un-
reliable, probably, are told of Horrocks, the
young man charged with the Hemmant (Q.)
murder. Amongst others, that he had a great
fancy for slaughtering fowls. If he sighted any
in tlie yard they would soon be dead. He was
down the bay in a small sailing-yacht, some time
since, and one of the crew fell overboard. They
were beating at the time, with Horrocks at the
tiller. lie looked round to the man in the water
with, “ I will pick you up next board.” The man
was never sighted again. “He’ll be all right;
he’s gone above,’’said Horrocks, whose demeanour
was always considered eccentric.
A Broken River (Vic.) squatter is rather

made the slight error of giving first prize in a
shorthorn bull class to a “stag ” which had been
penned up by mistake.

Press critics are puzzled over Rosebery.
He is pre-eminently an Imperialist, yet calls him-
self a Home-Ruler. He is President of the
Imperial Federation League and is (or was) Chair-
man of the Radical London County Council. He
was a staunch supporter of Gladstone when he
married, but Beaconsfield gave him his wife at the
altar. He is a chum of Wales and yet poses as a
friend of John Burns.

General Booth, it is cabled, announces
that the subscriptions to his “ submerged tenth ”
movement since the beginning of the year have
been only .£4OOO, and the great work is at a stand-
still. Consequently the vanguard of the vast,
unwashed army of thieves and cranks and lunatics
won >t appear on the Darling Downs just yet.
Booth says nothing about the .£lOO,OOO or so which
he scooped in last year to keep the “submerged
tenth.” According to the preacher in the
flaming garment, the contributions have fallen
off enormously this year in all departments, and a
cold paralysis is coming over the drum and a
catalepsy is upon the trombone as a consequence,
and the howl is frozen in the expounder’s throat
for want of funds, and the outlook is very, very
gloomy.
At a recent U.S. Negro Conference a
speaker said :
The black man wants everything he can get on erediti
not thinking of the day of settlement. He carries a
heavy bundle, and, when he unloads it, it is a hoe and
plough and ignorance; the white man carries a light
bundle, and, when he unloads it, it is a pen and ink. The
negro must learn to bore with a small auger first.
Negroes were exhorted to apply educative in-
fluences to themselves, to learn trades, to raise
their own food, to keep out of debt, and to buy
land. If the 6,000,000 U.S. negroes once awake
to an earnest appreciation of this programme, the
day for a sudden and desperate settlement of the
“ colour question ” in America is not far distant.

Dear Bulletin,—l have seen it printed in
your paper that Deeming insured his life once at
Rockhampton His height is given there as
Ssflu., whereas he is now only oft. 4 2 m.
Would it be a good idea for the authorities to
search the insurance records for his personal
statement, and the medico’s report attached
thereto. That would give his height, chest
measurement, weight, complexion, &e., and come
as near identification as possible. A man doesn’t
shrink four inches in a few years on this planet,
unless he is badly scalped or has I,is legs cut off.—
Yours, &0., Cosmos.

w© in an, who works for
“sweaters"—in her own words,
for “men who got the work from
the shops, cut it out, and gave it
her to make”—told the S.A..
Shops and Factories Commis-
sion, the other day, that she had
to make shirts complete and
ready for the warehouse at 3ld,
each. Her usual hours of work
Were from 7.50 a.m., until mid-
night or 1 a.m. It took her 11
hours to finish a dozen shirts,
for -which she got 45., but then
she.had to find her own cotton,
which cost (Id. She herself
employed two girls, one at 9s. a
week and the other at 6s.
Button-holers were paid 2s. (id.
per week for the first three
months and ss. a week for the
next ■ three - months. The
“ sweaters ’’ who employed her
made 18s. a day profit.
A hitherto unpublished log
in the Deeming pile. Early in
1887 a man passing as Fred.
Bailey arrived in Melbourne
from Adelaide. He announced
himself as anxious to learn
plumbing or engineering, or
both, and a big Melbourne con-
tractor gave him a trial. Fred.
Bailey soon showed that he knew
more about the wcik than any
of his fellows, and was prized
accordingly. While in Mel-
bourne Mr. Bailey took unto
himself a wife, and they were
'seen together fairly often. After
a little time Fred, left Mel-
bourne alone. This is a true
item, and all we want to know
is how many slightly - raised
hearthstones are there in this
fair land of ours? The contractor
was busy elsewhere when Deem-
ing was being identified.

“General Gresser, Prefect of Police at
St. Petersburg,” says the cable, “ has been
poisoned by Nihilists, and is sinking rapidly.”
When anything goes wrong in Europe the
“respectable” press always announces that the
Nihilist, or the Socialist, or the Anarchist is the
cause of it. In the present instance, the news
simply means that Gresser may have been
poisoned, or he may only have an awful pain in
his stomach ; and if he is poisoned the Nihilists
may have done it, or it may have been done
by Ids wife, who wanted to marry the other-
fellow, or by his next heir, who wanted his cash,,
or by somebody who wanted his billet, or by
Government which suspected him of being a-,
Nihilist himself, or he may have suicided, or his:
dinner may have been under-cooked. Meanwhile,,
later advices announce that he is only ill throughi
swallowing the prescriptions of a quack doctor off
the Sam Hood variety, whom he called in ; andl
further on it will turn out that lie is quite well,
and then that he died ten years ago, and after
that it will transpire that there never was such a
person at all, and finally ho will be sent to
Siberia. Also, just as we are going to press we
bear that he is dead, and is slowly recovering, and
that ho has been promoted, and has hanged him-
self, and has just got married, and is short in his
cash and gone crazy.
AN ANNUAL SUBSCRIBER.
Though wealthy squatters sweat the land
And sixpence would deny
To one who pleads with outstretched hand—
They cannot help but die ;
Yea, though their lives be meanly pass’t
In hoarding up the booty,
The Government chips in at last
And claims the probate duty.
In Old Mortality we find
The country’s truest friend :
Through him the rich assist mankind
And serve a useful end.
’Tis true he takes the good and great,
And spares not blushing beauty—
But by the State, as sure as fate,
He does his probate-duty.

AUTHORITY
NOW READY.
THE HISTORICAL RECORDS
OP
NEW SOUTH WALES.
Part 2 op Vol. I.
Demy Bvo. 747 Pages.
Published in conjunction with
THE HISTORY OF NEW SOUTH WALES
FROM THE RECORDS.
Tuts volume is the first of a series in which it ia
intended to print in extenso the official and semi-
official despatches and other records which con-
stitute the Archives of the Colony. It is intended
to accompany the “History of New South Wales
from the Records,” Vol. II of which is now in
course of preparation.
The greatest c -e lias been taken to secure
copies of every document of importance in the
Record Office and Departments of State in London,
the British Museum, and similar institutions,
bearing on the History of New South Wales.
The volume now ready covers the period 1783
to 1792, and will be found to contain numerous
authentic documents of the greatest interest,
hitherto unpublished. It will be followed by
Part 1 as soon as possible after the receipt of
certain information, now on its way from Eng-
land, relating to Captain Cook.
As it is anticipated that the series will form the
material on which future historians must largely
depend, and will throw a great deal of light on a
period of our History of which little is known, it
has been decided to offer it to the public at the
following very low prices, viz. :
To Subscribers —
Cloth, Gs. Gd.; Half Morocco, 10s. 6d.
To Non-subscribers —
Cloth, 7s. 6d.; Half Morocco, 12s. Gd.
*#* Post free within the Colony.
' CHARLES POTTER,
Government Printer.
Government Printing Office,
12th May, 1892.

A strange case concerning a lost infant is
reported from Hull (Eng.). In October last, tbe
plaintiff asserts, she sent her baby to church to be
christened, about six female neighbours being in
charge of the offspring. Their children also joined
the procession, and a strange woman, heavily
veiled, whom nobody could recognise, presently
attached herself to the caravan, and volunteered to
carry the precious infant. The result was that
the crowd came back with no infant, and presented
a slipshod report to the effect that they had
stopped to gossip with various other females, and
had “dropped in ’’somewhere for refreshments,
and when they dropped out again they became
aware that the strange woman and the baby had
both vanished. About three months later a girl
who had accompanied the party seized upon a
woman and an infant in the street, and gave them
b >th into custody as the missing pair. In court
bith women claimed the baby and tried to drag it
from each other, and both called evidence as to
the ownership, and both went into hysterics.
Moreover, it was asserted that the baby was the
living image of both women, and as nobody had
seen the kidnapper with her veil raised, and as
I the straggling caravan at the christening had been
taking refreshments as it went along, the identifi-
cation was very incomplete. Also, the defendant
had possessed an infant of her own, and if this
particular infant was not hers, then her own one
was altogether unaccounted for. Nevertheless,
the court finally adjudged the child to the plain-
tiff, and sent defendant to gaol for twelve months.
At latest advices she was fast going mad in prison,
and crying night and day for her lost daughter.
I There is no agitation for her release up to date,
and the feeling heart of England doesn’t sympa-
thise worth a cent.

mo THEATRICAL MANAGERS AND OTHERS.—The
J- figure “ALT,” with Latest Improvements, is now
open for Engagements here and elsewhere, Novelty for
World’s Pair. Address: 03 Botany Street, Darlinghurst,
Sydney. [Extract from the Era, November 13, 1880: —
The Automaton “ALI” during the lost month has been
on view at Collins’s Music Hall, Islington. A new Auto-
maton which the Inventor, A. De Lacy, has termed
“ ALI,” following custom immortal; the figure is dressed
in a turban and Oriental costume, it is worked by elabo-
rate wire works, the motive power being electricity. The
movements are natural and lifelike, and paradoxical
as it may seem it has not the appearance of being an
Automaton. “ALI” turns the leaves of the Koran,
becomes warlike and goes through sword exercise, plays
tambourine and bells, and altogether completely succeeds
in engrossing the attention of the audience, in interesting
them, and winning loud applause. The whole network of
wire, by which the movements of the figure are deter-
mined, is exposed by the Inventor, Mr. De Lacy, who
must have taken years to have brought his mechanical
figure to perfection, *

TOMBSTONES ON WHEELS'
“Jarvies,” and carriers are proverbial for their
ready wit and gift of repartee. This fact found
an illustration a few days since in a little incident
that occurred on the Old South Head Road. A
pedlar who officered a rather mysterious looking
conveyance was urging his jaded steed along the
highway, when he overtook an itinerant brother,
and the following conversation ensued ; “ Hallo 1
what do you carry in that craft, mate?” “Drugs
and medicines,” growled pedlar number one.
“ Then you’d better keep ahead of me, ’’ was the
facetious rejoinder, “ I’m loaded with tomb-
stones.”
The joke was a grim one, though singularly
enough we not infrequently hear stories in which
death, drugs, and the doctor are associated as
intimate connections. Yet when disease or
accident occurs, a man’s first thought, almost, is
to send for the doctor, excepting when the mis-
fortune or malady is of a character such as can be
successfully treated without medical assistance.
A sprained ancle, a severe bruise or burn, or a
ricked back, may be easily and effectually treated
by any one with the aid of St. Jacobs Oil. Its use
is also most valuable in cases of rheumatism,
neuralgia, sciatica, and numerous other affections
of the muscles and nerves. In the cure of any of
these complaints it completely supersedes medical
skill, and comes to the top victorious when the
patient has been “ given up ” by the doctor.
St. Jacobs Oil is sold by chemists and dealers
everywhere. Beware of worthless imitations.
DOM ESTIC ECONOMY.
The art of economising is one that, like all
others, may be cultivated. Economy does not
imply “pinching” or niggardliness. There is a
wide difference between true economy and the
spurious quality that demands the denial of
reasonable luxuries and the pleasures of a comfort-
able existence. It is the miser who afflicts him-
self, and sins against Nature, in order that he
may hoard up wealth —most probably to be spent
in prodigality after his death. Prudence and a
judicious management of capital—the avoidance
of reckless or unnecessary expenditure are
principal amongst the elements of true economy.
As a practical illustration let it be assumed that a
man wishes to buy a watch. What he needs to
look to is, firstly, that the watch is a good time-
keeper ; secondly, that it has a presentable
appearance; and thirdly (the above qualities
being undeniably assured), that the price is
reasonably within his means. Now, there is
but one class of watches in the world that fits
this description, and that is the Waterbury. The
Waterburys are par excellence “ the people’s time-
keepers,” a fact that every wearer of them cheer-
fully acknowledges. They are manufactured to
suit all incomes and every class of purchaser.
Series “E,” the old long-wind favorite, a tho-
roughly reliable and serviceable watch, costs only
13s. 6d. Series “J” (gent’s size), and “L” (ladies’
size), are retailed at 225. 6d., and, amongst other
improvements, are fitted with short-winding and
stem-setting appliances and enamelled dials.
More highly finished still are the latest additions
to the Waterbury family, series “ K ” and “N,”
for gentlemen and ladies respectively. These can
be obtained in nickel, sterling silver, or rolled
gold (with 15 years’ guarantee). In appearance,
finish, and utility they are incomparable, while
they are offered at prices that must astonish every-
body.

A Bathurst (N.S. W.) gag: “He don’t
know enough to take his coat off at tucker-time.”
The vessel that brought “Mad Fred.”
Deeming to Australia itself bore a mad name—
Kaiser Wilhelm 11.
“ When we understand what animals say,
I don’t suppose we shall be particularly flattered
by their opinions of us,” suggests G. R. Sims.
A Contemporary Heview writer reckons
up the Emperor of Germany as a man governed
by “ the spirit of crass self-advertising egotism.”
Advertisement in a Perth (W.A.) morning
paper :
("IHINESE, Coolies, Cooks, Black-boys and other
merchandise indented.
A Georgetown (N.Q.) paper boasts that the
town honoured Governor Norman with “a down-
right honest sort of a banquet, not a toddy, shoddy
banquet with a little salad backed up with a little
corned-beef.” We know the kind of thing—scrub
turkey, kangaroo, pickled alligator, stuffed emu,
roast hedgehog, soused snake, grilled gohanna,
minced lizards, bullock, salt mutton, sucking-pig,
wild boar, Chow-chow d la pigtail, kookaburra
giblets, dugong haras, and sardines ; washed down
with 30 o.p. methylated bug-juice, “jump-up”
diluted with rum, concentrated “ oil-of-bluestone”
(the famous baecy-and-kerosene blend), “strike-
me-dead ” whisky, and liquid dynamite.
Nicholas Zarouboff, a Russian “ secret
service officer ” of many years’ experience, is
spending his old age in writing his biography.
Zarouboff preceded the late Czar on his visit to
London, to keep an eye on any Polish exiles or
other malcontents who might want to pot that
potentate, and had nearly a hundred of them
“removed out of the way” as a precautionary
measure. How they were removed isn’t definitely
stated, but it would appear that they were arrested
by the obliging London police force on all
manner of charges, and either sentenced to short
terms in gaol, or kept under remand until the
good, old Czar was safe on his way home again.
Zarouboff’s best performance, however, was
some years afterwards. Two Russian “suspects”
a man and a woman, had fled to London, the
woman taking with her some papers which the
Russian authorities particularly desired to recover.
Zarouboff followed them, and hiring four mus-
cular bullies from a private enquiry office, he
drove down in open daylight to their hotel.
There he assumed an official aspect, announced to
the manager that he was a detective armed witli
an extradition warrant, and after exhibiting a
document covered with Slavonic hieroglyphics he
walked up to the room where the two “suspects ”
were at breakfast. They could speak no English,
and their wild I’emonstrances in Russian and
Polish were set down as the howls of two escaped
lunatics. Zarouboff and Ids party handcuffed the
pair, gagged them by shoving a piece of wood in the
mouth of each, paid the bill, carried the prisoners
downstairs, threw them into a cab, and drove
away. Then the active officer took them to his
own house and poured brandy into them till they
were both drunk, after which they were placed on
board a steamer and conveyed to St. Petersburg,
where they both vanished, probably to Siberia or
else among the damp dungeons of the fortress of
St. Peter and Paul. On the voyage the two
prisoners tried vociferously to explain how they
had been kidnapped, but Zarouboff simply in-
formed the captain that they were a pair of
escaped maniacs and that he was their authorised
keeper, and after that nobody listened to them.
“In England,” says the affable Nicholas, “you
may seize upon anybody, carry him anywhere,
and do anything with him, only by declaring
without a shadow of evidence that he is a
lunatic.”
“Bouey” was the principal converted
nigger of a certain N.Q. town. He was an in-
valuable darkey who spoke good English, and
never got drunk, and was the right hand man of
the local publican—in short, he was a wondrous
specimen of exotic civilisation. In the same town
lived John Stalker, caretaker of the local Botanic
Gardens, and a friend and patron of the elevated
aboriginal. Stalker was agent for a number of
Southern museums, and when he got an order for
any tropical curiosities he generally passed tne
commission on to Boney, and when the article was
forthcoming he paid that deputy with two sticks
of tobacco, and sent in his own bill for
£5. The scheme worked well till one day an
order arrived for an aboriginal skull —it was
wanted to complete an unfinished skeleton, and
Stalker was requested to use the utmost dispatch.
Therefore he impressed on the faithful Boney that
the required specimen must be on hand by Mon-
day, and Boney cheerfully undertook to be
punctual. On Sunday he invited “Combo,” the
magistrate’s nigger, to come out fishing with him,
and that lost, misguided heathen went and was
never seen again. But late on Sunday night
Boney trotted cheerfully into Stalker’s house
with a bag hanging from his shoulder.
Out of the bag he extracted a gore-stained
head which Thing of Horror he deposited on
Stalker’s table right among his supper. The care-
taker of the local Botanic Gardens looked at it
for just one instant, and with a yell he climbed
madly over the saved aboriginal in his haste to
get out through the door. By-and-bye he yelled
himself out and came back. The Awful Object
was still there among his victuals, and with
chattering teeth he asked Boney where it
came from. That lost native actually laughed.
“Me bin take Combo ’long fishin,” he ex-
plained cheerfully; “cut him head off.” Then
Stalker seized Boney in a frantic grasp and
howled softly in his ear that if he didn’t get
out of the door on the instant, and run for his life
till he got right to the other end of the continent,
and change his name, and repent of his sin, and
never go out in the day-time for the rest of his
life, he would assuredly be taken and hanged, and
damned after being hanged, and be pursued by
debbil-debbil for ever and ever. Whereupon
Boney awoke to a sense of his danger, and not
even waiting to collect the tobacco due to him, he
rushed out into the night and was never seen again
in those parts. And when ho was gone, Stalker
carefully boiled Combo’s head till the flesh came
off, and bis bill for “One skull, .£5,” was paid in
due course. The other part of Combo was never
found.

The Scott, Church of England chap-
lain to Melbourne gaol, and spiritual adviser by
appointment to F. B. Deeming, objects to the
conduct of Marshall Lyle, in attempting to come
between the condemned gentleman and his lawful
shepherd. Just as he (Mr. Scott) had started to
hug the delusion that Deeming was getting into
“ a more suitable frame of mind,” his misguided
attorney suggested Dr. Strong as a good man for
the soul, whereupon the prisoner became unsettled
and showed signs of wanting to do without any
parson at all. But Mr. Scott, bless his heart !
intends to hang on to the doubtful penitent until
he drops, and asks nothing more than to be left
alone with his prey. If necessary, he will apply
for a Supreme Court injunction to restrain Deem-
ing from confessing his sins to Dr. Strong, or to
anybody else other than the faithful Scott, whose
son, or nephew, runs a little weekly rag at Foots-
cray (Melb.) and has promised the constant
readers a special edition of revelations anon—
Friend Lyle, whilst the shepherd dear
His prisoner “prepares,”
Keep right away—don’t interfere
In heavenly affairs.
You are no agent for the Loard
(Who loves attorneys not),
And, now that Deeming's fairly floored,
Just leave his soul to Scott.
We grant you did your little best
In battle with the rope,
And Deeming up among the blest
Will sing of you, we hope ;
But Heaven itself is faraway,
Whilst h—l is very hot,
And that’s the reason why we say—
Do leave his soul to Scott. •
The chaplain angles day by day,
And casts his line at night,
And very soon, bethinks, he may
Expect to feel a bite ;
His bait it is the Holy Book ;
What joy when he has got
That canny fish upon the hook-*
Another soul to Scott!
Good Mister Lyle, you have a nerve
To talk of fetching Strong
To poach upon this rook’s preserve
Where he has fished so long.
We will admit your pastor is
A big salvatory pot,
But this is purely prison biz.,
So leave it all to Scott.
We do not call your conduct mean,
But ill-advised and vain,
On Deeming’s togs you have a lien,
The doctors get liis brain ;
His body to the Law will go
(That’s quite another lot),
But everybody ought to know
His soul belongs to Scott.

Tales of the Early Days.
BY PRICE WARUNG.
No. Vll.—The Falling of the Doom.
(Fob; The Bulletin.)
The secret society of the Ring had, in
regular conclave, * ordered that Brother William
Felix, No. 20, of “Nine Circle,” should within
one lunar month, stab or shoot to the death
Brother Henry Reynell, No. 12, of “ Seven
Circle.” Reynell’s offence was (as already related)
the promising “to be true man” to Civil Com-
mandant Maconochie. Convict Bill Felix was
a member of the sub-gang of which Convict Henry
Reynell was the leader ; and, inasmuch as Reynell
had chosen Felix to be a member of 5 B Farm sub-
gang, thus freeing him from the constant wearing
of fetters and conferring upon him a desirable
degree of freedom, Felix had sworn to be his
(Reynell’s) man “ for ever and a day.” The tie of
fraternity which linked Reynell and Felix thus
was sadly complicated with the obligation of
obedience which bound the latter to the Ring.
Let Felix obey the Ring, and he would have to
enact the doom upon the one soul for whom he
cared. Let him refuse to execute the death-
warrant issued under the seal of the “One”—tfSh
dread head or “ Centre ” of the Society—and the
doom he refused to Reynell would be his own.
The Ring having given over someone to the doom,
would demand the life of the appointed executioner
if he failed within the specified time to complete
his task. In rare instances a regulation or law of
the Society might be modified or altered in effect.
But never in its history had there been known a
case where a death-warrant had been left unful-
filled and the stated executioner had continued to
live. The idol would demand appeasement for
its lust, if not in the person of one victim, in
another’s.
There is an impressive story as to the working of
this Medean law. Before the existing “ One ” it
is believed three men had filled the awful office.
The second in the administration had been ordered
to murder the then Commandant, Captain Wright.
He had acquiesced in the need for the crime-
otherwise theTrder would not have been ratified.
And, as the “ One,” it was his duty to perform the
doom on the Commandant. It was a minor but still
immutable law of the Ring that the “old man”
should only die by the “One’s” hands. The
honour was accorded to him as a privilege of his
dignity. Yet Captain Wright lived to be Major
and to give evidence before the Select Committee
on Transportation of the House of Commons.
How was that ? ,
Wright had been suddenly recalled to t Sydney.
The vessel which brought the summons of recall
could not lie off the harbourless island in the
storm-season for longer than a week, and iristant
preparations for his departure were set on foot by
the Commandant. The news spread—and twice
within the week was his life attempted in vain.
He got on board the vessel safe; thus unknow-
ingly he committed the “One” to the wrath
and vengeance of the Ring; and the Ring
demanded its vicarious sacrifice. Three days
after Wright’s sailing the body of one of the most
intelligent of the “free” constables was found
suspended from a tall pine. The dead man was
supposed to have been in pretty general favour with
the transports and his fellow officers ; hence it was
not believed that he had been murdered, and his
death was attributed to suicide. The military
surgeon, who made an examination of the corpse,
drew tire attention of the subaltern of the guard to
a curious symbol burnt or tattooed into the flesh
of the chest and freshly
cut across with a knife.
The scarification was,
however, only skin-
deep, and had been
done after death. The
officers did not recog-
nise at the moment the
significance of the scarf
It was the symbol of
the “ One ” :
Not even the dreaded Head of the Society was
free of its penalties.
# # *
Civil-Commandant Maconochie (it will be
remembered) had, in his anxiety- to acquire a
knowledge of the Ring’s methods of communica-
tion, been trapped into conveying the report, of
how certain “Ringers” had voted at the trial
of Reynell. “ Condemnation or Acquittal ?”—the
question hung thus in the balance when Macbno-
chie had appeared in the yard where the Ring
Lodge was in session of denunciation. Nine were
for condemnation —for sending on the accused to
the “ Conclave of Doombut thirteen votes were
required by the law of the secret society before
the condemnation could be passed. And four votes
Macdriochie had been trapped into conveying.
Without knowing the precise bearings of his
action, he had learnt enough to understand that
he had given Reynell over to the doom. An
interjection by a Ringer who was a loyal friend
to Reynell—strange, how in this accursed com-
munity of felonry, which a noble member of the
House , of Lords stated to be deficient in every
human attribute, feelings of affection refused to
absolutely die out, and thus prove his lordship
right ! —had informed Maconochie of so much.
What was the doom : death, mutilation, or a
simple “sending to Coventry?” Maconochie
asked several of the officers of the Establishment,
but could gain no satisfactory answer. “ Most
likely death!” he was told by the gaoler. .“The
Ring didn’t think much o’ death !”
Herein the gaoler was subject to that tendency
to error which infected all thoughts and beliefs,
of whatever nature, held in the University of
Depravity.J The Ring thought a good deal of
death when that Mighty Leveller was enlisted
on their behalf. It was only when Death
acted for the Authorities that they snapped
their fingers in his , face and jested plea-
santly with him. When the Ring used him, he
was to its members an instrument of terror, and
they surrounded him in their imaginations with
every ghastly, every agonising, every horrific
attribute, of which the distorted culture of the
society’s founders, or the dark fancies of the most
ignorant Ringers—such as those who ever trembled
at the verge of madness—could invent and adaptl
But, so momentous is the alteration in human
feeling, which can be effected by changing the
point of view, Death had but to draw his fees
from the Establishment to be sneered at, ridi-
culed, and derisively welcomed. Black Norfolkers
went sardonically to the grave at the Establish-
ment's orders, just because the Establishment
wished them to do differently.
* # .#
Maconochie sent for Johnson, leader at the
session of denunciation.
“Have you any objection, sir, to relate the
precise significance of the condemnation which
you understand the Ring has passed on prisoner
Reynell ?”
“ ’Eaps !” was the laconic rejoinder.
“I beg your pardon ! What did you say ?”
“ ’Eaps ! I sed I ’av ’eaps of objecshuns.”
“Oh !” Then, after a pause, “I believe, John-
son, you have been a prisoner under the Crown
for many years?”
“ More’n can count!”
“Yes? Then you must have heard read many
times the regulation as to answering truly and
explicitly, and without prevarication or evasion
or denial, all questions put by persons in properly-
constituted authority?”
“ Can’t say as I ’av, yer Honor !”
“Johnson!”
“ Yes, yer Honor ? ”
“I mean to deal fairly and kindly with every man
on the island—but I will have truth-speaking. I
never forgive a lie, except it is uttered under the
influence of terror ! ”
“ In wot ’av’ I lied, yer Honor ? ”
“ You said you had never heard the regulation
enforcing ”
“ Savin’ yer Honor’s presence, I said nothink o’
the kind! Yer arsked me ’ad I ’erd it read.
Well, I never did ? I’ve ’erd it mumbled ev’ry
Sunday since I was a kinchin—but never ’erd it
read wunst. There ain’t no ’stablishment orf’cer
as can read—unless it’s yerself.” The rascal
grinned in enjoyment of his own satire,
“ You know the meaning of the regulation—
what it enforces —however ? ”
“ O’ course : to answer th’ truth, th’ ’ole truth
an’ nothink but th’ truth w’en ’terrogated by
’stablishment orf’cer.”
“ Then answer me, sir.” (Not imperatively,
but with a studied politeness, did Maconochie
now speak,) “ What judgment—what ‘ doom ’as
you call it—has your society ordered upon Rey-
nell?” . - •
Johnson gazed reflectively at the; ceiling. He
passed his right hand over the corrugations of his
forehead, and drew it down the. scarred and
weather-blighted cheeks to the stern, square jowl
that had gripped numberless groans of agony in
their utterance, arid bid them be dumb. Then he
said : , ■ ■ j.. ;, ■
“Mr. Com’dant, Pa’son- Taylor - tells us that
w’en th’ higher law conflicts wi’ th’ lower, we must
alius obey th’ higher—alius th' higher. Do th’'
pa’son’s views meet wi’ yer approval, sir ?” •
The Commandant, already i once trapped ;by
Johnson, was dubious of the fair seeming of the
interrogation, arid declined to answer directly.
“Answer.my question!”
“ Wi’ orl respecks, y’r Honor, I can’t till I know
wot to obey—that as is th’ higher law or that as is
th’ lower!”
“There is no question of higher or lower law
here, my man—none. It is merely a matter of
answering my question. What is Reyuell’s doom?”
“ That’s w’ere yer an’ me jest differ, y’r Honor.
’Tis orl a matter .o’ higher an’ lower law. If I
•answer-tlri question, I obey th’ law o’ th’ System.
If I don’t answer it, then I obey th’ law o’ th’
Ring, an’ I’d ’av y’r Honor know as fur me an’
secli as me ’tis th’ King’s,law as is highest law.”
Again the fellow’s lip's parted arid his cheeks
.wrinkled in a gleeful defiance of 'Authority.
“You’re talking foolishly,” rejoined the Com-
mandant, bearing the implied taunt with a
patience of tone arid fnanner that, if. lie had only
known it, was more’likely to penetrate to Johri-
'son’s bet’tei’ nature than any number of Authority-
phrased words; “You’re' twisting Mr. Taylor’s
sayings to suit your own purpose. 'Mr. Taylor
meant, no doubt, that when human law conflicts
with the moral law of conscience or revealed law,
then the latter, as the higher law, must be obeyed.”
No more unfortunate admission could have been
made by a System’s officer; and the ingenious
Johnson, whose naturally-sharp wits the attrition
of adversity had ground.to remarkable keenness,
while wearing away the moral part of him, eagerly
seized the opportunity thus offered of making an
embarrassing criticism on the System.
“ That’s jest it, y’r Honor—that’s th’ very
identical thing as I mean. Now, th’ System’s
laws an’ reg’lashuns is th’ lower law, an’ our laws
an’ reg’lashuns—th’Ring’s laws, that is—they’re
th’ higher, ’cos . But will yer ’ear th’ reason,
yer Honor ’”
“ Go on—though you are talking insubordinate
nonsense. I Will hear what you have to say ! ”
“ This is th’ reason. Th’ Ring’s law is th’
moral law ’cos it’s founded on justice ! ” He
stooped and, placing his hands on his knees,
crooked his head so as to glare impishly into the
Commandant’s face to watch the effect of his
words, or rather of those he left unsaid.
For not what the wretch said but what he left
unsaid stung the Commandant. The implication
was clear. The System was not founded upon
justice. And in his heart of hearts Maconochie
knew the accusation was true. Penalties British
law justly provided for those who offended against
it, but then British law proposed only to punish,
and not to give over the offenders to “ unusual
punishment” and utter corruption. The System
did this, however—and the taunt went home. But,
what could Maconochie do ? Argument imperilled
his.authority, arid, after all, he did not invent the
System. So: —
“You decline to answer what is Reynell’s
doom ?”
“ Aye, y’r Honor, ’cos th’ Ring forbids me!”
“You know I can inflict penalties upon you for
refusing to answer my plain interrogatory ?”
“Short o’puttin’me into an ’oss’ necklace, yer
can, sir. But yer won’t punish me !”
.“Why?” Against his judgment, the Com-
mandant put the enquiry. Similar remarks had
been made to him before by men up for punish-
ment, but invariably they had been uttered in
suppliant or cringing tones. This fellow, however,
spoke with the confidence of knowledge.
“ W’y ? ‘Cos yer know wot I ses is true. An’
: ’cos, although yer an orf’cer o’ th’Systum, yer
’art ’tain’t in the Systum’s way o’ doin’ things.
That’s w’y, sir. Yer ain’t been long ’nuff ’ere to
.’a’ changed th’ ’art o’ a man fur th’ ’art o’ a beast.
Yer know who said that, y’r Honor?’
*See The Bulletin— “ Tales of the Early'Days,”|Nos.
IV., V., and Vl.—“The Convening of the Ring,” “The-'
Session of Denunciation,” and “The Conclave of Doom,”
+ It is supposed that the incident here related was the
origin of an order once issued by John Price by which
every “bond” arid “free” constable was required to
present himself stripped for examination by a medical
officer at certain periods. The “bond” constables had
no alternative but obedience, but the “free”, officers,
almost to a'man, refused to'submit to periodical inspection
as degrading. They appealed to the Governor in Van
Diemen’s Land, who upheld the objection, in their case,
“ till Mr. Price could show specific reason for the pro-
posed course in the interests of discipline.” Price replied
in (for him) a remarkably indiscreet manner—in what
was known as a “Semi-official communication”—by
alleging that it was necessary to find out from time to
time “ whether officers’ personal marks varied.” There-
upon Mr. Price received one of the few snubs of his
official career!
{ “University of Depravity.” Archbishop Whately,
in his speech to the House of Lords, of May, 1840, on his
motion for the abolition of Transportation, thus described
Norfolk Island.
